10150-8{_}3{_}|       |                                                      |       +------------------------------------------------------+      DRACULA'S GUEST  by  BRAM STOKER  First published 1914        To  MY SON     CONTENTS        Dracula's Guest                                  9      The Judge's House                               26      The Squaw                                       50      The Secret of the Growing Gold                  67      The Gipsy Prophecy                              84      The Coming of Abel Behenna                      96      The Burial of the Rats                         120      A Dream of Red Hands                           152      Crooken Sands                                  165     PREFACE   A few months before the lamented death of my husband--I might say even as the shadow of death was over him--he planned three series of short stories for publication, and the present volume is one of them.
10150-8{_}4{_}To his original list of stories in this book, I have added an hitherto unpublished episode from _Dracula_.
10150-8{_}5{_}It was originally excised owing to the length of the book, and may prove of interest to the many readers of what is considered my husband's most remarkable work.
10150-8{_}6{_}The other stories have already been published in English and American periodicals.
10150-8{_}7{_}Had my husband lived longer, he might have seen fit to revise this work, which is mainly from the earlier years of his strenuous life.
10150-8{_}8{_}But, as fate has entrusted to me the issuing of it, I consider it fitting and proper to let it go forth practically as it was left by him.
10150-8{_}9{_}FLORENCE BRAM STOKER     Dracula's Guest   When we started for our drive the sun was shining brightly on Munich, and the air was full of the joyousness of early summer.
10150-8{_}10{_}Just as we were about to depart, Herr Delbrück (the maître d'hôtel of the Quatre Saisons, where I was staying) came down, bareheaded, to the carriage and, after wishing me a pleasant drive, said to the coachman, still holding his hand on the handle of the carriage door:  'Remember you are back by nightfall.
10150-8{_}11{_}The sky looks bright but there is a shiver in the north wind that says there may be a sudden storm.
10150-8{_}12{_}But I am sure you will not be late.'
10150-8{_}13{_}Here he smiled, and added, 'for you know what night it is.'
10150-8{_}14{_}Johann answered with an emphatic, 'Ja, mein Herr,' and, touching his hat, drove off quickly.
10150-8{_}15{_}When we had cleared the town, I said, after signalling to him to stop:  'Tell me, Johann, what is tonight?'
10150-8{_}16{_}He crossed himself, as he answered laconically: 'Walpurgis nacht.'
10150-8{_}17{_}Then he took out his watch, a great, old-fashioned German silver thing as big as a turnip, and looked at it, with his eyebrows gathered together and a little impatient shrug of his shoulders.
10150-8{_}18{_}I realised that this was his way of respectfully protesting against the unnecessary delay, and sank back in the carriage, merely motioning him to proceed.
10150-8{_}19{_}He started off rapidly, as if to make up for lost time.
10150-8{_}20{_}Every now and then the horses seemed to throw up their heads and sniffed the air suspiciously.
10150-8{_}21{_}On such occasions I often looked round in alarm.
10150-8{_}22{_}The road was pretty bleak, for we were traversing a sort of high, wind-swept plateau.
10150-8{_}23{_}As we drove, I saw a road that looked but little used, and which seemed to dip through a little, winding valley.
10150-8{_}24{_}It looked so inviting that, even at the risk of offending him, I called Johann to stop--and when he had pulled up, I told him I would like to drive down that road.
10150-8{_}25{_}He made all sorts of excuses, and frequently crossed himself as he spoke.
10150-8{_}26{_}This somewhat piqued my curiosity, so I asked him various questions.
10150-8{_}27{_}He answered fencingly, and repeatedly looked at his watch in protest.
10150-8{_}28{_}Finally I said:  'Well, Johann, I want to go down this road.
10150-8{_}29{_}I shall not ask you to come unless you like; but tell me why you do not like to go, that is all I ask.'
10150-8{_}30{_}For answer he seemed to throw himself off the box, so quickly did he reach the ground.
10150-8{_}31{_}Then he stretched out his hands appealingly to me, and implored me not to go.
10150-8{_}32{_}There was just enough of English mixed with the German for me to understand the drift of his talk.
10150-8{_}33{_}He seemed always just about to tell me something--the very idea of which evidently frightened him; but each time he pulled himself up, saying, as he crossed himself: 'Walpurgis-Nacht!'
10150-8{_}34{_}I tried to argue with him, but it was difficult to argue with a man when I did not know his language.
10150-8{_}35{_}The advantage certainly rested with him, for although he began to speak in English, of a very crude and broken kind, he always got excited and broke into his native tongue--and every time he did so, he looked at his watch.
10150-8{_}36{_}Then the horses became restless and sniffed the air.
10150-8{_}37{_}At this he grew very pale, and, looking around in a frightened way, he suddenly jumped forward, took them by the bridles and led them on some twenty feet.
10150-8{_}38{_}I followed, and asked why he had done this.
10150-8{_}39{_}For answer he crossed himself, pointed to the spot we had left and drew his carriage in the direction of the other road, indicating a cross, and said, first in German, then in English: 'Buried him--him what killed themselves.'
10150-8{_}40{_}I remembered the old custom of burying suicides at cross-roads: 'Ah!
10150-8{_}41{_}I see, a suicide.
10150-8{_}42{_}How interesting!'
10150-8{_}43{_}But for the life of me I could not make out why the horses were frightened.
10150-8{_}44{_}Whilst we were talking, we heard a sort of sound between a yelp and a bark.
10150-8{_}45{_}It was far away; but the horses got very restless, and it took Johann all his time to quiet them.
10150-8{_}46{_}He was pale, and said, 'It sounds like a wolf--but yet there are no wolves here now.'
10150-8{_}47{_}'No?'
10150-8{_}48{_}I said, questioning him; 'isn't it long since the wolves were so near the city?'
10150-8{_}49{_}'Long, long,' he answered, 'in the spring and summer; but with the snow the wolves have been here not so long.'
10150-8{_}50{_}Whilst he was petting the horses and trying to quiet them, dark clouds drifted rapidly across the sky.
10150-8{_}51{_}The sunshine passed away, and a breath of cold wind seemed to drift past us.
10150-8{_}52{_}It was only a breath, however, and more in the nature of a warning than a fact, for the sun came out brightly again.
10150-8{_}53{_}Johann looked under his lifted hand at the horizon and said:  'The storm of snow, he comes before long time.'
10150-8{_}54{_}Then he looked at his watch again, and, straightway holding his reins firmly--for the horses were still pawing the ground restlessly and shaking their heads--he climbed to his box as though the time had come for proceeding on our journey.
10150-8{_}55{_}I felt a little obstinate and did not at once get into the carriage.
10150-8{_}56{_}'Tell me,' I said, 'about this place where the road leads,' and I pointed down.
10150-8{_}57{_}Again he crossed himself and mumbled a prayer, before he answered, 'It is unholy.'
10150-8{_}58{_}'What is unholy?'
10150-8{_}59{_}I enquired.
10150-8{_}60{_}'The village.'
10150-8{_}61{_}'Then there is a village?'
10150-8{_}62{_}'No, no.
10150-8{_}63{_}No one lives there hundreds of years.'
10150-8{_}64{_}My curiosity was piqued, 'But you said there was a village.'
10150-8{_}65{_}'There was.'
10150-8{_}66{_}'Where is it now?'
10150-8{_}67{_}Whereupon he burst out into a long story in German and English, so mixed up that I could not quite understand exactly what he said, but roughly I gathered that long ago, hundreds of years, men had died there and been buried in their graves; and sounds were heard under the clay, and when the graves were opened, men and women were found rosy with life, and their mouths red with blood.
10150-8{_}68{_}And so, in haste to save their lives (aye, and their souls!--and here he crossed himself) those who were left fled away to other places, where the living lived, and the dead were dead and not--not something.
10150-8{_}69{_}He was evidently afraid to speak the last words.
10150-8{_}70{_}As he proceeded with his narration, he grew more and more excited.
10150-8{_}71{_}It seemed as if his imagination had got hold of him, and he ended in a perfect paroxysm of fear--white-faced, perspiring, trembling and looking round him, as if expecting that some dreadful presence would manifest itself there in the bright sunshine on the open plain.
10150-8{_}72{_}Finally, in an agony of desperation, he cried:  'Walpurgis nacht!'
10150-8{_}73{_}and pointed to the carriage for me to get in.
10150-8{_}74{_}All my English blood rose at this, and, standing back, I said:  'You are afraid, Johann--you are afraid.
10150-8{_}75{_}Go home; I shall return alone; the walk will do me good.'
10150-8{_}76{_}The carriage door was open.
10150-8{_}77{_}I took from the seat my oak walking-stick--which I always carry on my holiday excursions--and closed the door, pointing back to Munich, and said, 'Go home, Johann--Walpurgis-nacht doesn't concern Englishmen.'
10150-8{_}78{_}The horses were now more restive than ever, and Johann was trying to hold them in, while excitedly imploring me not to do anything so foolish.
10150-8{_}79{_}I pitied the poor fellow, he was deeply in earnest; but all the same I could not help laughing.
10150-8{_}80{_}His English was quite gone now.
10150-8{_}81{_}In his anxiety he had forgotten that his only means of making me understand was to talk my language, so he jabbered away in his native German.
10150-8{_}82{_}It began to be a little tedious.
10150-8{_}83{_}After giving the direction, 'Home!'
10150-8{_}84{_}I turned to go down the cross-road into the valley.
10150-8{_}85{_}With a despairing gesture, Johann turned his horses towards Munich.
10150-8{_}86{_}I leaned on my stick and looked after him.
10150-8{_}87{_}He went slowly along the road for a while: then there came over the crest of the hill a man tall and thin.
10150-8{_}88{_}I could see so much in the distance.
10150-8{_}89{_}When he drew near the horses, they began to jump and kick about, then to scream with terror.
10150-8{_}90{_}Johann could not hold them in; they bolted down the road, running away madly.
10150-8{_}91{_}I watched them out of sight, then looked for the stranger, but I found that he, too, was gone.
10150-8{_}92{_}With a light heart I turned down the side road through the deepening valley to which Johann had objected.
10150-8{_}93{_}There was not the slightest reason, that I could see, for his objection; and I daresay I tramped for a couple of hours without thinking of time or distance, and certainly without seeing a person or a house.
10150-8{_}94{_}So far as the place was concerned, it was desolation itself.
10150-8{_}95{_}But I did not notice this particularly till, on turning a bend in the road, I came upon a scattered fringe of wood; then I recognised that I had been impressed unconsciously by the desolation of the region through which I had passed.
10150-8{_}96{_}I sat down to rest myself, and began to look around.
10150-8{_}97{_}It struck me that it was considerably colder than it had been at the commencement of my walk--a sort of sighing sound seemed to be around me, with, now and then, high overhead, a sort of muffled roar.
10150-8{_}98{_}Looking upwards I noticed that great thick clouds were drifting rapidly across the sky from North to South at a great height.
10150-8{_}99{_}There were signs of coming storm in some lofty stratum of the air.
10150-8{_}100{_}I was a little chilly, and, thinking that it was the sitting still after the exercise of walking, I resumed my journey.
10150-8{_}101{_}The ground I passed over was now much more picturesque.
10150-8{_}102{_}There were no striking objects that the eye might single out; but in all there was a charm of beauty.
10150-8{_}103{_}I took little heed of time and it was only when the deepening twilight forced itself upon me that I began to think of how I should find my way home.
10150-8{_}104{_}The brightness of the day had gone.
10150-8{_}105{_}The air was cold, and the drifting of clouds high overhead was more marked.
10150-8{_}106{_}They were accompanied by a sort of far-away rushing sound, through which seemed to come at intervals that mysterious cry which the driver had said came from a wolf.
10150-8{_}107{_}For a while I hesitated.
10150-8{_}108{_}I had said I would see the deserted village, so on I went, and presently came on a wide stretch of open country, shut in by hills all around.
10150-8{_}109{_}Their sides were covered with trees which spread down to the plain, dotting, in clumps, the gentler slopes and hollows which showed here and there.
10150-8{_}110{_}I followed with my eye the winding of the road, and saw that it curved close to one of the densest of these clumps and was lost behind it.
10150-8{_}111{_}As I looked there came a cold shiver in the air, and the snow began to fall.
10150-8{_}112{_}I thought of the miles and miles of bleak country I had passed, and then hurried on to seek the shelter of the wood in front.
10150-8{_}113{_}Darker and darker grew the sky, and faster and heavier fell the snow, till the earth before and around me was a glistening white carpet the further edge of which was lost in misty vagueness.
10150-8{_}114{_}The road was here but crude, and when on the level its boundaries were not so marked, as when it passed through the cuttings; and in a little while I found that I must have strayed from it, for I missed underfoot the hard surface, and my feet sank deeper in the grass and moss.
10150-8{_}115{_}Then the wind grew stronger and blew with ever increasing force, till I was fain to run before it.
10150-8{_}116{_}The air became icy-cold, and in spite of my exercise I began to suffer.
10150-8{_}117{_}The snow was now falling so thickly and whirling around me in such rapid eddies that I could hardly keep my eyes open.
10150-8{_}118{_}Every now and then the heavens were torn asunder by vivid lightning, and in the flashes I could see ahead of me a great mass of trees, chiefly yew and cypress all heavily coated with snow.
10150-8{_}119{_}I was soon amongst the shelter of the trees, and there, in comparative silence, I could hear the rush of the wind high overhead.
10150-8{_}120{_}Presently the blackness of the storm had become merged in the darkness of the night.
10150-8{_}121{_}By-and-by the storm seemed to be passing away: it now only came in fierce puffs or blasts.
10150-8{_}122{_}At such moments the weird sound of the wolf appeared to be echoed by many similar sounds around me.
10150-8{_}123{_}Now and again, through the black mass of drifting cloud, came a straggling ray of moonlight, which lit up the expanse, and showed me that I was at the edge of a dense mass of cypress and yew trees.
10150-8{_}124{_}As the snow had ceased to fall, I walked out from the shelter and began to investigate more closely.
10150-8{_}125{_}It appeared to me that, amongst so many old foundations as I had passed, there might be still standing a house in which, though in ruins, I could find some sort of shelter for a while.
10150-8{_}126{_}As I skirted the edge of the copse, I found that a low wall encircled it, and following this I presently found an opening.
10150-8{_}127{_}Here the cypresses formed an alley leading up to a square mass of some kind of building.
10150-8{_}128{_}Just as I caught sight of this, however, the drifting clouds obscured the moon, and I passed up the path in darkness.
10150-8{_}129{_}The wind must have grown colder, for I felt myself shiver as I walked; but there was hope of shelter, and I groped my way blindly on.
10150-8{_}130{_}I stopped, for there was a sudden stillness.
10150-8{_}131{_}The storm had passed; and, perhaps in sympathy with nature's silence, my heart seemed to cease to beat.
10150-8{_}132{_}But this was only momentarily; for suddenly the moonlight broke through the clouds, showing me that I was in a graveyard, and that the square object before me was a great massive tomb of marble, as white as the snow that lay on and all around it.
10150-8{_}133{_}With the moonlight there came a fierce sigh of the storm, which appeared to resume its course with a long, low howl, as of many dogs or wolves.
10150-8{_}134{_}I was awed and shocked, and felt the cold perceptibly grow upon me till it seemed to grip me by the heart.
10150-8{_}135{_}Then while the flood of moonlight still fell on the marble tomb, the storm gave further evidence of renewing, as though it was returning on its track.
10150-8{_}136{_}Impelled by some sort of fascination, I approached the sepulchre to see what it was, and why such a thing stood alone in such a place.
10150-8{_}137{_}I walked around it, and read, over the Doric door, in German:                 COUNTESS DOLINGEN OF GRATZ                         IN STYRIA                  SOUGHT AND FOUND DEATH                           1801  On the top of the tomb, seemingly driven through the solid marble--for the structure was composed of a few vast blocks of stone--was a great iron spike or stake.
10150-8{_}138{_}On going to the back I saw, graven in great Russian letters:            'The dead travel fast.'
10150-8{_}139{_}There was something so weird and uncanny about the whole thing that it gave me a turn and made me feel quite faint.
10150-8{_}140{_}I began to wish, for the first time, that I had taken Johann's advice.
10150-8{_}141{_}Here a thought struck me, which came under almost mysterious circumstances and with a terrible shock.
10150-8{_}142{_}This was Walpurgis Night!
10150-8{_}143{_}Walpurgis Night, when, according to the belief of millions of people, the devil was abroad--when the graves were opened and the dead came forth and walked.
10150-8{_}144{_}When all evil things of earth and air and water held revel.
10150-8{_}145{_}This very place the driver had specially shunned.
10150-8{_}146{_}This was the depopulated village of centuries ago.
10150-8{_}147{_}This was where the suicide lay; and this was the place where I was alone--unmanned, shivering with cold in a shroud of snow with a wild storm gathering again upon me!
10150-8{_}148{_}It took all my philosophy, all the religion I had been taught, all my courage, not to collapse in a paroxysm of fright.
10150-8{_}149{_}And now a perfect tornado burst upon me.
10150-8{_}150{_}The ground shook as though thousands of horses thundered across it; and this time the storm bore on its icy wings, not snow, but great hailstones which drove with such violence that they might have come from the thongs of Balearic slingers--hailstones that beat down leaf and branch and made the shelter of the cypresses of no more avail than though their stems were standing-corn.
10150-8{_}151{_}At the first I had rushed to the nearest tree; but I was soon fain to leave it and seek the only spot that seemed to afford refuge, the deep Doric doorway of the marble tomb.
10150-8{_}152{_}There, crouching against the massive bronze door, I gained a certain amount of protection from the beating of the hailstones, for now they only drove against me as they ricocheted from the ground and the side of the marble.
10150-8{_}153{_}As I leaned against the door, it moved slightly and opened inwards.
10150-8{_}154{_}The shelter of even a tomb was welcome in that pitiless tempest, and I was about to enter it when there came a flash of forked-lightning that lit up the whole expanse of the heavens.
10150-8{_}155{_}In the instant, as I am a living man, I saw, as my eyes were turned into the darkness of the tomb, a beautiful woman, with rounded cheeks and red lips, seemingly sleeping on a bier.
10150-8{_}156{_}As the thunder broke overhead, I was grasped as by the hand of a giant and hurled out into the storm.
10150-8{_}157{_}The whole thing was so sudden that, before I could realise the shock, moral as well as physical, I found the hailstones beating me down.
10150-8{_}158{_}At the same time I had a strange, dominating feeling that I was not alone.
10150-8{_}159{_}I looked towards the tomb.
10150-8{_}160{_}Just then there came another blinding flash, which seemed to strike the iron stake that surmounted the tomb and to pour through to the earth, blasting and crumbling the marble, as in a burst of flame.
10150-8{_}161{_}The dead woman rose for a moment of agony, while she was lapped in the flame, and her bitter scream of pain was drowned in the thundercrash.
10150-8{_}162{_}The last thing I heard was this mingling of dreadful sound, as again I was seized in the giant-grasp and dragged away, while the hailstones beat on me, and the air around seemed reverberant with the howling of wolves.
10150-8{_}163{_}The last sight that I remembered was a vague, white, moving mass, as if all the graves around me had sent out the phantoms of their sheeted-dead, and that they were closing in on me through the white cloudiness of the driving hail.
10150-8{_}164{_}*       *       *       *       *  Gradually there came a sort of vague beginning of consciousness; then a sense of weariness that was dreadful.
10150-8{_}165{_}For a time I remembered nothing; but slowly my senses returned.
10150-8{_}166{_}My feet seemed positively racked with pain, yet I could not move them.
10150-8{_}167{_}They seemed to be numbed.
10150-8{_}168{_}There was an icy feeling at the back of my neck and all down my spine, and my ears, like my feet, were dead, yet in torment; but there was in my breast a sense of warmth which was, by comparison, delicious.
10150-8{_}169{_}It was as a nightmare--a physical nightmare, if one may use such an expression; for some heavy weight on my chest made it difficult for me to breathe.
10150-8{_}170{_}This period of semi-lethargy seemed to remain a long time, and as it faded away I must have slept or swooned.
10150-8{_}171{_}Then came a sort of loathing, like the first stage of sea-sickness, and a wild desire to be free from something--I knew not what.
10150-8{_}172{_}A vast stillness enveloped me, as though all the world were asleep or dead--only broken by the low panting as of some animal close to me.
10150-8{_}173{_}I felt a warm rasping at my throat, then came a consciousness of the awful truth, which chilled me to the heart and sent the blood surging up through my brain.
10150-8{_}174{_}Some great animal was lying on me and now licking my throat.
10150-8{_}175{_}I feared to stir, for some instinct of prudence bade me lie still; but the brute seemed to realise that there was now some change in me, for it raised its head.
10150-8{_}176{_}Through my eyelashes I saw above me the two great flaming eyes of a gigantic wolf.
10150-8{_}177{_}Its sharp white teeth gleamed in the gaping red mouth, and I could feel its hot breath fierce and acrid upon me.
10150-8{_}178{_}For another spell of time I remembered no more.
10150-8{_}179{_}Then I became conscious of a low growl, followed by a yelp, renewed again and again.
10150-8{_}180{_}Then, seemingly very far away, I heard a 'Holloa!
10150-8{_}181{_}holloa!'
10150-8{_}182{_}as of many voices calling in unison.
10150-8{_}183{_}Cautiously I raised my head and looked in the direction whence the sound came; but the cemetery blocked my view.
10150-8{_}184{_}The wolf still continued to yelp in a strange way, and a red glare began to move round the grove of cypresses, as though following the sound.
10150-8{_}185{_}As the voices drew closer, the wolf yelped faster and louder.
10150-8{_}186{_}I feared to make either sound or motion.
10150-8{_}187{_}Nearer came the red glow, over the white pall which stretched into the darkness around me.
10150-8{_}188{_}Then all at once from beyond the trees there came at a trot a troop of horsemen bearing torches.
10150-8{_}189{_}The wolf rose from my breast and made for the cemetery.
10150-8{_}190{_}I saw one of the horsemen (soldiers by their caps and their long military cloaks) raise his carbine and take aim.
10150-8{_}191{_}A companion knocked up his arm, and I heard the ball whizz over my head.
10150-8{_}192{_}He had evidently taken my body for that of the wolf.
10150-8{_}193{_}Another sighted the animal as it slunk away, and a shot followed.
10150-8{_}194{_}Then, at a gallop, the troop rode forward--some towards me, others following the wolf as it disappeared amongst the snow-clad cypresses.
10150-8{_}195{_}As they drew nearer I tried to move, but was powerless, although I could see and hear all that went on around me.
10150-8{_}196{_}Two or three of the soldiers jumped from their horses and knelt beside me.
10150-8{_}197{_}One of them raised my head, and placed his hand over my heart.
10150-8{_}198{_}'Good news, comrades!'
10150-8{_}199{_}he cried.
10150-8{_}200{_}'His heart still beats!'
10150-8{_}201{_}Then some brandy was poured down my throat; it put vigour into me, and I was able to open my eyes fully and look around.
10150-8{_}202{_}Lights and shadows were moving among the trees, and I heard men call to one another.
10150-8{_}203{_}They drew together, uttering frightened exclamations; and the lights flashed as the others came pouring out of the cemetery pell-mell, like men possessed.
10150-8{_}204{_}When the further ones came close to us, those who were around me asked them eagerly:  'Well, have you found him?'
10150-8{_}205{_}The reply rang out hurriedly:  'No!
10150-8{_}206{_}no!
10150-8{_}207{_}Come away quick--quick!
10150-8{_}208{_}This is no place to stay, and on this of all nights!'
10150-8{_}209{_}'What was it?'
10150-8{_}210{_}was the question, asked in all manner of keys.
10150-8{_}211{_}The answer came variously and all indefinitely as though the men were moved by some common impulse to speak, yet were restrained by some common fear from giving their thoughts.
10150-8{_}212{_}'It--it--indeed!'
10150-8{_}213{_}gibbered one, whose wits had plainly given out for the moment.
10150-8{_}214{_}'A wolf--and yet not a wolf!'
10150-8{_}215{_}another put in shudderingly.
10150-8{_}216{_}'No use trying for him without the sacred bullet,' a third remarked in a more ordinary manner.
10150-8{_}217{_}'Serve us right for coming out on this night!
10150-8{_}218{_}Truly we have earned our thousand marks!'
10150-8{_}219{_}were the ejaculations of a fourth.
10150-8{_}220{_}'There was blood on the broken marble,' another said after a pause--'the lightning never brought that there.
10150-8{_}221{_}And for him--is he safe?
10150-8{_}222{_}Look at his throat!
10150-8{_}223{_}See, comrades, the wolf has been lying on him and keeping his blood warm.'
10150-8{_}224{_}The officer looked at my throat and replied:  'He is all right; the skin is not pierced.
10150-8{_}225{_}What does it all mean?
10150-8{_}226{_}We should never have found him but for the yelping of the wolf.'
10150-8{_}227{_}'What became of it?'
10150-8{_}228{_}asked the man who was holding up my head, and who seemed the least panic-stricken of the party, for his hands were steady and without tremor.
10150-8{_}229{_}On his sleeve was the chevron of a petty officer.
10150-8{_}230{_}'It went to its home,' answered the man, whose long face was pallid, and who actually shook with terror as he glanced around him fearfully.
10150-8{_}231{_}'There are graves enough there in which it may lie.
10150-8{_}232{_}Come, comrades--come quickly!
10150-8{_}233{_}Let us leave this cursed spot.'
10150-8{_}234{_}The officer raised me to a sitting posture, as he uttered a word of command; then several men placed me upon a horse.
10150-8{_}235{_}He sprang to the saddle behind me, took me in his arms, gave the word to advance; and, turning our faces away from the cypresses, we rode away in swift, military order.
10150-8{_}236{_}As yet my tongue refused its office, and I was perforce silent.
10150-8{_}237{_}I must have fallen asleep; for the next thing I remembered was finding myself standing up, supported by a soldier on each side of me.
10150-8{_}238{_}It was almost broad daylight, and to the north a red streak of sunlight was reflected, like a path of blood, over the waste of snow.
10150-8{_}239{_}The officer was telling the men to say nothing of what they had seen, except that they found an English stranger, guarded by a large dog.
10150-8{_}240{_}'Dog!
10150-8{_}241{_}that was no dog,' cut in the man who had exhibited such fear.
10150-8{_}242{_}'I think I know a wolf when I see one.'
10150-8{_}243{_}The young officer answered calmly: 'I said a dog.'
10150-8{_}244{_}'Dog!'
10150-8{_}245{_}reiterated the other ironically.
10150-8{_}246{_}It was evident that his courage was rising with the sun; and, pointing to me, he said, 'Look at his throat.
10150-8{_}247{_}Is that the work of a dog, master?'
10150-8{_}248{_}Instinctively I raised my hand to my throat, and as I touched it I cried out in pain.
10150-8{_}249{_}The men crowded round to look, some stooping down from their saddles; and again there came the calm voice of the young officer:  'A dog, as I said.
10150-8{_}250{_}If aught else were said we should only be laughed at.'
10150-8{_}251{_}I was then mounted behind a trooper, and we rode on into the suburbs of Munich.
10150-8{_}252{_}Here we came across a stray carriage, into which I was lifted, and it was driven off to the Quatre Saisons--the young officer accompanying me, whilst a trooper followed with his horse, and the others rode off to their barracks.
10150-8{_}253{_}When we arrived, Herr Delbrück rushed so quickly down the steps to meet me, that it was apparent he had been watching within.
10150-8{_}254{_}Taking me by both hands he solicitously led me in.
10150-8{_}255{_}The officer saluted me and was turning to withdraw, when I recognised his purpose, and insisted that he should come to my rooms.
10150-8{_}256{_}Over a glass of wine I warmly thanked him and his brave comrades for saving me.
10150-8{_}257{_}He replied simply that he was more than glad, and that Herr Delbrück had at the first taken steps to make all the searching party pleased; at which ambiguous utterance the maître d'hôtel smiled, while the officer pleaded duty and withdrew.
10150-8{_}258{_}'But Herr Delbrück,' I enquired, 'how and why was it that the soldiers searched for me?'
10150-8{_}259{_}He shrugged his shoulders, as if in depreciation of his own deed, as he replied:  'I was so fortunate as to obtain leave from the commander of the regiment in which I served, to ask for volunteers.'
10150-8{_}260{_}'But how did you know I was lost?'
10150-8{_}261{_}I asked.
10150-8{_}262{_}'The driver came hither with the remains of his carriage, which had been upset when the horses ran away.'
10150-8{_}263{_}'But surely you would not send a search-party of soldiers merely on this account?'
10150-8{_}264{_}'Oh, no!'
10150-8{_}265{_}he answered; 'but even before the coachman arrived, I had this telegram from the Boyar whose guest you are,' and he took from his pocket a telegram which he handed to me, and I read:                                                    _Bistritz_.
10150-8{_}266{_}Be careful of my guest--his safety is most precious to me.
10150-8{_}267{_}Should aught happen to him, or if he be missed, spare nothing      to find him and ensure his safety.
10150-8{_}268{_}He is English and therefore      adventurous.
10150-8{_}269{_}There are often dangers from snow and wolves and      night.
10150-8{_}270{_}Lose not a moment if you suspect harm to him.
10150-8{_}271{_}I answer      your zeal with my fortune.--_Dracula_.
10150-8{_}272{_}As I held the telegram in my hand, the room seemed to whirl around me; and, if the attentive maître d'hôtel had not caught me, I think I should have fallen.
10150-8{_}273{_}There was something so strange in all this, something so weird and impossible to imagine, that there grew on me a sense of my being in some way the sport of opposite forces--the mere vague idea of which seemed in a way to paralyse me.
10150-8{_}274{_}I was certainly under some form of mysterious protection.
10150-8{_}275{_}From a distant country had come, in the very nick of time, a message that took me out of the danger of the snow-sleep and the jaws of the wolf.
10150-8{_}276{_}The Judge's House   When the time for his examination drew near Malcolm Malcolmson made up his mind to go somewhere to read by himself.
10150-8{_}277{_}He feared the attractions of the seaside, and also he feared completely rural isolation, for of old he knew it charms, and so he determined to find some unpretentious little town where there would be nothing to distract him.
10150-8{_}278{_}He refrained from asking suggestions from any of his friends, for he argued that each would recommend some place of which he had knowledge, and where he had already acquaintances.
10150-8{_}279{_}As Malcolmson wished to avoid friends he had no wish to encumber himself with the attention of friends' friends, and so he determined to look out for a place for himself.
10150-8{_}280{_}He packed a portmanteau with some clothes and all the books he required, and then took ticket for the first name on the local time-table which he did not know.
10150-8{_}281{_}When at the end of three hours' journey he alighted at Benchurch, he felt satisfied that he had so far obliterated his tracks as to be sure of having a peaceful opportunity of pursuing his studies.
10150-8{_}282{_}He went straight to the one inn which the sleepy little place contained, and put up for the night.
10150-8{_}283{_}Benchurch was a market town, and once in three weeks was crowded to excess, but for the remainder of the twenty-one days it was as attractive as a desert.
10150-8{_}284{_}Malcolmson looked around the day after his arrival to try to find quarters more isolated than even so quiet an inn as 'The Good Traveller' afforded.
10150-8{_}285{_}There was only one place which took his fancy, and it certainly satisfied his wildest ideas regarding quiet; in fact, quiet was not the proper word to apply to it--desolation was the only term conveying any suitable idea of its isolation.
10150-8{_}286{_}It was an old rambling, heavy-built house of the Jacobean style, with heavy gables and windows, unusually small, and set higher than was customary in such houses, and was surrounded with a high brick wall massively built.
10150-8{_}287{_}Indeed, on examination, it looked more like a fortified house than an ordinary dwelling.
10150-8{_}288{_}But all these things pleased Malcolmson.
10150-8{_}289{_}'Here,' he thought, 'is the very spot I have been looking for, and if I can get opportunity of using it I shall be happy.'
10150-8{_}290{_}His joy was increased when he realised beyond doubt that it was not at present inhabited.
10150-8{_}291{_}From the post-office he got the name of the agent, who was rarely surprised at the application to rent a part of the old house.
10150-8{_}292{_}Mr. Carnford, the local lawyer and agent, was a genial old gentleman, and frankly confessed his delight at anyone being willing to live in the house.
10150-8{_}293{_}'To tell you the truth,' said he, 'I should be only too happy, on behalf of the owners, to let anyone have the house rent free for a term of years if only to accustom the people here to see it inhabited.
10150-8{_}294{_}It has been so long empty that some kind of absurd prejudice has grown up about it, and this can be best put down by its occupation--if only,' he added with a sly glance at Malcolmson, 'by a scholar like yourself, who wants its quiet for a time.'
10150-8{_}295{_}Malcolmson thought it needless to ask the agent about the 'absurd prejudice'; he knew he would get more information, if he should require it, on that subject from other quarters.
10150-8{_}296{_}He paid his three months' rent, got a receipt, and the name of an old woman who would probably undertake to 'do' for him, and came away with the keys in his pocket.
10150-8{_}297{_}He then went to the landlady of the inn, who was a cheerful and most kindly person, and asked her advice as to such stores and provisions as he would be likely to require.
10150-8{_}298{_}She threw up her hands in amazement when he told her where he was going to settle himself.
10150-8{_}299{_}'Not in the Judge's House!'
10150-8{_}300{_}she said, and grew pale as she spoke.
10150-8{_}301{_}He explained the locality of the house, saying that he did not know its name.
10150-8{_}302{_}When he had finished she answered:  'Aye, sure enough--sure enough the very place!
10150-8{_}303{_}It is the Judge's House sure enough.'
10150-8{_}304{_}He asked her to tell him about the place, why so called, and what there was against it.
10150-8{_}305{_}She told him that it was so called locally because it had been many years before--how long she could not say, as she was herself from another part of the country, but she thought it must have been a hundred years or more--the abode of a judge who was held in great terror on account of his harsh sentences and his hostility to prisoners at Assizes.
10150-8{_}306{_}As to what there was against the house itself she could not tell.
10150-8{_}307{_}She had often asked, but no one could inform her; but there was a general feeling that there was _something_, and for her own part she would not take all the money in Drinkwater's Bank and stay in the house an hour by herself.
10150-8{_}308{_}Then she apologised to Malcolmson for her disturbing talk.
10150-8{_}309{_}'It is too bad of me, sir, and you--and a young gentlemen, too--if you will pardon me saying it, going to live there all alone.
10150-8{_}310{_}If you were my boy--and you'll excuse me for saying it--you wouldn't sleep there a night, not if I had to go there myself and pull the big alarm bell that's on the roof!'
10150-8{_}311{_}The good creature was so manifestly in earnest, and was so kindly in her intentions, that Malcolmson, although amused, was touched.
10150-8{_}312{_}He told her kindly how much he appreciated her interest in him, and added:  'But, my dear Mrs. Witham, indeed you need not be concerned about me!
10150-8{_}313{_}A man who is reading for the Mathematical Tripos has too much to think of to be disturbed by any of these mysterious "somethings", and his work is of too exact and prosaic a kind to allow of his having any corner in his mind for mysteries of any kind.
10150-8{_}314{_}Harmonical Progression, Permutations and Combinations, and Elliptic Functions have sufficient mysteries for me!'
10150-8{_}315{_}Mrs. Witham kindly undertook to see after his commissions, and he went himself to look for the old woman who had been recommended to him.
10150-8{_}316{_}When he returned to the Judge's House with her, after an interval of a couple of hours, he found Mrs. Witham herself waiting with several men and boys carrying parcels, and an upholsterer's man with a bed in a car, for she said, though tables and chairs might be all very well, a bed that hadn't been aired for mayhap fifty years was not proper for young bones to lie on.
10150-8{_}317{_}She was evidently curious to see the inside of the house; and though manifestly so afraid of the 'somethings' that at the slightest sound she clutched on to Malcolmson, whom she never left for a moment, went over the whole place.
10150-8{_}318{_}After his examination of the house, Malcolmson decided to take up his abode in the great dining-room, which was big enough to serve for all his requirements; and Mrs. Witham, with the aid of the charwoman, Mrs. Dempster, proceeded to arrange matters.
10150-8{_}319{_}When the hampers were brought in and unpacked, Malcolmson saw that with much kind forethought she had sent from her own kitchen sufficient provisions to last for a few days.
10150-8{_}320{_}Before going she expressed all sorts of kind wishes; and at the door turned and said:  'And perhaps, sir, as the room is big and draughty it might be well to have one of those big screens put round your bed at night--though, truth to tell, I would die myself if I were to be so shut in with all kinds of--of "things", that put their heads round the sides, or over the top, and look on me!'
10150-8{_}321{_}The image which she had called up was too much for her nerves, and she fled incontinently.
10150-8{_}322{_}Mrs. Dempster sniffed in a superior manner as the landlady disappeared, and remarked that for her own part she wasn't afraid of all the bogies in the kingdom.
10150-8{_}323{_}'I'll tell you what it is, sir,' she said; 'bogies is all kinds and sorts of things--except bogies!
10150-8{_}324{_}Rats and mice, and beetles; and creaky doors, and loose slates, and broken panes, and stiff drawer handles, that stay out when you pull them and then fall down in the middle of the night.
10150-8{_}325{_}Look at the wainscot of the room!
10150-8{_}326{_}It is old--hundreds of years old!
10150-8{_}327{_}Do you think there's no rats and beetles there!
10150-8{_}328{_}And do you imagine, sir, that you won't see none of them?
10150-8{_}329{_}Rats is bogies, I tell you, and bogies is rats; and don't you get to think anything else!'
10150-8{_}330{_}'Mrs.
10150-8{_}331{_}Dempster,' said Malcolmson gravely, making her a polite bow, 'you know more than a Senior Wrangler!
10150-8{_}332{_}And let me say, that, as a mark of esteem for your indubitable soundness of head and heart, I shall, when I go, give you possession of this house, and let you stay here by yourself for the last two months of my tenancy, for four weeks will serve my purpose.'
10150-8{_}333{_}'Thank you kindly, sir!'
10150-8{_}334{_}she answered, 'but I couldn't sleep away from home a night.
10150-8{_}335{_}I am in Greenhow's Charity, and if I slept a night away from my rooms I should lose all I have got to live on.
10150-8{_}336{_}The rules is very strict; and there's too many watching for a vacancy for me to run any risks in the matter.
10150-8{_}337{_}Only for that, sir, I'd gladly come here and attend on you altogether during your stay.'
10150-8{_}338{_}'My good woman,' said Malcolmson hastily, 'I have come here on purpose to obtain solitude; and believe me that I am grateful to the late Greenhow for having so organised his admirable charity--whatever it is--that I am perforce denied the opportunity of suffering from such a form of temptation!
10150-8{_}339{_}Saint Anthony himself could not be more rigid on the point!'
10150-8{_}340{_}The old woman laughed harshly.
10150-8{_}341{_}'Ah, you young gentlemen,' she said, 'you don't fear for naught; and belike you'll get all the solitude you want here.'
10150-8{_}342{_}She set to work with her cleaning; and by nightfall, when Malcolmson returned from his walk--he always had one of his books to study as he walked--he found the room swept and tidied, a fire burning in the old hearth, the lamp lit, and the table spread for supper with Mrs. Witham's excellent fare.
10150-8{_}343{_}'This is comfort, indeed,' he said, as he rubbed his hands.
10150-8{_}344{_}When he had finished his supper, and lifted the tray to the other end of the great oak dining-table, he got out his books again, put fresh wood on the fire, trimmed his lamp, and set himself down to a spell of real hard work.
10150-8{_}345{_}He went on without pause till about eleven o'clock, when he knocked off for a bit to fix his fire and lamp, and to make himself a cup of tea.
10150-8{_}346{_}He had always been a tea-drinker, and during his college life had sat late at work and had taken tea late.
10150-8{_}347{_}The rest was a great luxury to him, and he enjoyed it with a sense of delicious, voluptuous ease.
10150-8{_}348{_}The renewed fire leaped and sparkled, and threw quaint shadows through the great old room; and as he sipped his hot tea he revelled in the sense of isolation from his kind.
10150-8{_}349{_}Then it was that he began to notice for the first time what a noise the rats were making.
10150-8{_}350{_}'Surely,' he thought, 'they cannot have been at it all the time I was reading.
10150-8{_}351{_}Had they been, I must have noticed it!'
10150-8{_}352{_}Presently, when the noise increased, he satisfied himself that it was really new.
10150-8{_}353{_}It was evident that at first the rats had been frightened at the presence of a stranger, and the light of fire and lamp; but that as the time went on they had grown bolder and were now disporting themselves as was their wont.
10150-8{_}354{_}How busy they were!
10150-8{_}355{_}and hark to the strange noises!
10150-8{_}356{_}Up and down behind the old wainscot, over the ceiling and under the floor they raced, and gnawed, and scratched!
10150-8{_}357{_}Malcolmson smiled to himself as he recalled to mind the saying of Mrs. Dempster, 'Bogies is rats, and rats is bogies!'
10150-8{_}358{_}The tea began to have its effect of intellectual and nervous stimulus, he saw with joy another long spell of work to be done before the night was past, and in the sense of security which it gave him, he allowed himself the luxury of a good look round the room.
10150-8{_}359{_}He took his lamp in one hand, and went all around, wondering that so quaint and beautiful an old house had been so long neglected.
10150-8{_}360{_}The carving of the oak on the panels of the wainscot was fine, and on and round the doors and windows it was beautiful and of rare merit.
10150-8{_}361{_}There were some old pictures on the walls, but they were coated so thick with dust and dirt that he could not distinguish any detail of them, though he held his lamp as high as he could over his head.
10150-8{_}362{_}Here and there as he went round he saw some crack or hole blocked for a moment by the face of a rat with its bright eyes glittering in the light, but in an instant it was gone, and a squeak and a scamper followed.
10150-8{_}363{_}The thing that most struck him, however, was the rope of the great alarm bell on the roof, which hung down in a corner of the room on the right-hand side of the fireplace.
10150-8{_}364{_}He pulled up close to the hearth a great high-backed carved oak chair, and sat down to his last cup of tea.
10150-8{_}365{_}When this was done he made up the fire, and went back to his work, sitting at the corner of the table, having the fire to his left.
10150-8{_}366{_}For a little while the rats disturbed him somewhat with their perpetual scampering, but he got accustomed to the noise as one does to the ticking of a clock or to the roar of moving water; and he became so immersed in his work that everything in the world, except the problem which he was trying to solve, passed away from him.
10150-8{_}367{_}He suddenly looked up, his problem was still unsolved, and there was in the air that sense of the hour before the dawn, which is so dread to doubtful life.
10150-8{_}368{_}The noise of the rats had ceased.
10150-8{_}369{_}Indeed it seemed to him that it must have ceased but lately and that it was the sudden cessation which had disturbed him.
10150-8{_}370{_}The fire had fallen low, but still it threw out a deep red glow.
10150-8{_}371{_}As he looked he started in spite of his _sang froid_.
10150-8{_}372{_}There on the great high-backed carved oak chair by the right side of the fireplace sat an enormous rat, steadily glaring at him with baleful eyes.
10150-8{_}373{_}He made a motion to it as though to hunt it away, but it did not stir.
10150-8{_}374{_}Then he made the motion of throwing something.
10150-8{_}375{_}Still it did not stir, but showed its great white teeth angrily, and its cruel eyes shone in the lamplight with an added vindictiveness.
10150-8{_}376{_}Malcolmson felt amazed, and seizing the poker from the hearth ran at it to kill it.
10150-8{_}377{_}Before, however, he could strike it, the rat, with a squeak that sounded like the concentration of hate, jumped upon the floor, and, running up the rope of the alarm bell, disappeared in the darkness beyond the range of the green-shaded lamp.
10150-8{_}378{_}Instantly, strange to say, the noisy scampering of the rats in the wainscot began again.
10150-8{_}379{_}By this time Malcolmson's mind was quite off the problem; and as a shrill cock-crow outside told him of the approach of morning, he went to bed and to sleep.
10150-8{_}380{_}He slept so sound that he was not even waked by Mrs. Dempster coming in to make up his room.
10150-8{_}381{_}It was only when she had tidied up the place and got his breakfast ready and tapped on the screen which closed in his bed that he woke.
10150-8{_}382{_}He was a little tired still after his night's hard work, but a strong cup of tea soon freshened him up and, taking his book, he went out for his morning walk, bringing with him a few sandwiches lest he should not care to return till dinner time.
10150-8{_}383{_}He found a quiet walk between high elms some way outside the town, and here he spent the greater part of the day studying his Laplace.
10150-8{_}384{_}On his return he looked in to see Mrs. Witham and to thank her for her kindness.
10150-8{_}385{_}When she saw him coming through the diamond-paned bay window of her sanctum she came out to meet him and asked him in.
10150-8{_}386{_}She looked at him searchingly and shook her head as she said:  'You must not overdo it, sir.
10150-8{_}387{_}You are paler this morning than you should be.
10150-8{_}388{_}Too late hours and too hard work on the brain isn't good for any man!
10150-8{_}389{_}But tell me, sir, how did you pass the night?
10150-8{_}390{_}Well, I hope?
10150-8{_}391{_}But my heart!
10150-8{_}392{_}sir, I was glad when Mrs. Dempster told me this morning that you were all right and sleeping sound when she went in.'
10150-8{_}393{_}'Oh, I was all right,' he answered smiling, 'the "somethings" didn't worry me, as yet.
10150-8{_}394{_}Only the rats; and they had a circus, I tell you, all over the place.
10150-8{_}395{_}There was one wicked looking old devil that sat up on my own chair by the fire, and wouldn't go till I took the poker to him, and then he ran up the rope of the alarm bell and got to somewhere up the wall or the ceiling--I couldn't see where, it was so dark.'
10150-8{_}396{_}'Mercy on us,' said Mrs. Witham, 'an old devil, and sitting on a chair by the fireside!
10150-8{_}397{_}Take care, sir!
10150-8{_}398{_}take care!
10150-8{_}399{_}There's many a true word spoken in jest.'
10150-8{_}400{_}'How do you mean?
10150-8{_}401{_}Pon my word I don't understand.'
10150-8{_}402{_}'An old devil!
10150-8{_}403{_}The old devil, perhaps.
10150-8{_}404{_}There!
10150-8{_}405{_}sir, you needn't laugh,' for Malcolmson had broken into a hearty peal.
10150-8{_}406{_}'You young folks thinks it easy to laugh at things that makes older ones shudder.
10150-8{_}407{_}Never mind, sir!
10150-8{_}408{_}never mind!
10150-8{_}409{_}Please God, you'll laugh all the time.
10150-8{_}410{_}It's what I wish you myself!'
10150-8{_}411{_}and the good lady beamed all over in sympathy with his enjoyment, her fears gone for a moment.
10150-8{_}412{_}'Oh, forgive me!'
10150-8{_}413{_}said Malcolmson presently.
10150-8{_}414{_}'Don't think me rude; but the idea was too much for me--that the old devil himself was on the chair last night!'
10150-8{_}415{_}And at the thought he laughed again.
10150-8{_}416{_}Then he went home to dinner.
10150-8{_}417{_}This evening the scampering of the rats began earlier; indeed it had been going on before his arrival, and only ceased whilst his presence by its freshness disturbed them.
10150-8{_}418{_}After dinner he sat by the fire for a while and had a smoke; and then, having cleared his table, began to work as before.
10150-8{_}419{_}Tonight the rats disturbed him more than they had done on the previous night.
10150-8{_}420{_}How they scampered up and down and under and over!
10150-8{_}421{_}How they squeaked, and scratched, and gnawed!
10150-8{_}422{_}How they, getting bolder by degrees, came to the mouths of their holes and to the chinks and cracks and crannies in the wainscoting till their eyes shone like tiny lamps as the firelight rose and fell.
10150-8{_}423{_}But to him, now doubtless accustomed to them, their eyes were not wicked; only their playfulness touched him.
10150-8{_}424{_}Sometimes the boldest of them made sallies out on the floor or along the mouldings of the wainscot.
10150-8{_}425{_}Now and again as they disturbed him Malcolmson made a sound to frighten them, smiting the table with his hand or giving a fierce 'Hsh, hsh,' so that they fled straightway to their holes.
10150-8{_}426{_}And so the early part of the night wore on; and despite the noise Malcolmson got more and more immersed in his work.
10150-8{_}427{_}All at once he stopped, as on the previous night, being overcome by a sudden sense of silence.
10150-8{_}428{_}There was not the faintest sound of gnaw, or scratch, or squeak.
10150-8{_}429{_}The silence was as of the grave.
10150-8{_}430{_}He remembered the odd occurrence of the previous night, and instinctively he looked at the chair standing close by the fireside.
10150-8{_}431{_}And then a very odd sensation thrilled through him.
10150-8{_}432{_}There, on the great old high-backed carved oak chair beside the fireplace sat the same enormous rat, steadily glaring at him with baleful eyes.
10150-8{_}433{_}Instinctively he took the nearest thing to his hand, a book of logarithms, and flung it at it.
10150-8{_}434{_}The book was badly aimed and the rat did not stir, so again the poker performance of the previous night was repeated; and again the rat, being closely pursued, fled up the rope of the alarm bell.
10150-8{_}435{_}Strangely too, the departure of this rat was instantly followed by the renewal of the noise made by the general rat community.
10150-8{_}436{_}On this occasion, as on the previous one, Malcolmson could not see at what part of the room the rat disappeared, for the green shade of his lamp left the upper part of the room in darkness, and the fire had burned low.
10150-8{_}437{_}On looking at his watch he found it was close on midnight; and, not sorry for the _divertissement_, he made up his fire and made himself his nightly pot of tea.
10150-8{_}438{_}He had got through a good spell of work, and thought himself entitled to a cigarette; and so he sat on the great oak chair before the fire and enjoyed it.
10150-8{_}439{_}Whilst smoking he began to think that he would like to know where the rat disappeared to, for he had certain ideas for the morrow not entirely disconnected with a rat-trap.
10150-8{_}440{_}Accordingly he lit another lamp and placed it so that it would shine well into the right-hand corner of the wall by the fireplace.
10150-8{_}441{_}Then he got all the books he had with him, and placed them handy to throw at the vermin.
10150-8{_}442{_}Finally he lifted the rope of the alarm bell and placed the end of it on the table, fixing the extreme end under the lamp.
10150-8{_}443{_}As he handled it he could not help noticing how pliable it was, especially for so strong a rope, and one not in use.
10150-8{_}444{_}'You could hang a man with it,' he thought to himself.
10150-8{_}445{_}When his preparations were made he looked around, and said complacently:  'There now, my friend, I think we shall learn something of you this time!'
10150-8{_}446{_}He began his work again, and though as before somewhat disturbed at first by the noise of the rats, soon lost himself in his propositions and problems.
10150-8{_}447{_}Again he was called to his immediate surroundings suddenly.
10150-8{_}448{_}This time it might not have been the sudden silence only which took his attention; there was a slight movement of the rope, and the lamp moved.
10150-8{_}449{_}Without stirring, he looked to see if his pile of books was within range, and then cast his eye along the rope.
10150-8{_}450{_}As he looked he saw the great rat drop from the rope on the oak arm-chair and sit there glaring at him.
10150-8{_}451{_}He raised a book in his right hand, and taking careful aim, flung it at the rat.
10150-8{_}452{_}The latter, with a quick movement, sprang aside and dodged the missile.
10150-8{_}453{_}He then took another book, and a third, and flung them one after another at the rat, but each time unsuccessfully.
10150-8{_}454{_}At last, as he stood with a book poised in his hand to throw, the rat squeaked and seemed afraid.
10150-8{_}455{_}This made Malcolmson more than ever eager to strike, and the book flew and struck the rat a resounding blow.
10150-8{_}456{_}It gave a terrified squeak, and turning on his pursuer a look of terrible malevolence, ran up the chair-back and made a great jump to the rope of the alarm bell and ran up it like lightning.
10150-8{_}457{_}The lamp rocked under the sudden strain, but it was a heavy one and did not topple over.
10150-8{_}458{_}Malcolmson kept his eyes on the rat, and saw it by the light of the second lamp leap to a moulding of the wainscot and disappear through a hole in one of the great pictures which hung on the wall, obscured and invisible through its coating of dirt and dust.
10150-8{_}459{_}'I shall look up my friend's habitation in the morning,' said the student, as he went over to collect his books.
10150-8{_}460{_}'The third picture from the fireplace; I shall not forget.'
10150-8{_}461{_}He picked up the books one by one, commenting on them as he lifted them.
10150-8{_}462{_}'_Conic Sections_ he does not mind, nor _Cycloidal Oscillations_, nor the _Principia_, nor _Quaternions_, nor _Thermodynamics_.
10150-8{_}463{_}Now for the book that fetched him!'
10150-8{_}464{_}Malcolmson took it up and looked at it.
10150-8{_}465{_}As he did so he started, and a sudden pallor overspread his face.
10150-8{_}466{_}He looked round uneasily and shivered slightly, as he murmured to himself:  'The Bible my mother gave me!
10150-8{_}467{_}What an odd coincidence.'
10150-8{_}468{_}He sat down to work again, and the rats in the wainscot renewed their gambols.
10150-8{_}469{_}They did not disturb him, however; somehow their presence gave him a sense of companionship.
10150-8{_}470{_}But he could not attend to his work, and after striving to master the subject on which he was engaged gave it up in despair, and went to bed as the first streak of dawn stole in through the eastern window.
10150-8{_}471{_}He slept heavily but uneasily, and dreamed much; and when Mrs. Dempster woke him late in the morning he seemed ill at ease, and for a few minutes did not seem to realise exactly where he was.
10150-8{_}472{_}His first request rather surprised the servant.
10150-8{_}473{_}'Mrs.
10150-8{_}474{_}Dempster, when I am out to-day I wish you would get the steps and dust or wash those pictures--specially that one the third from the fireplace--I want to see what they are.'
10150-8{_}475{_}Late in the afternoon Malcolmson worked at his books in the shaded walk, and the cheerfulness of the previous day came back to him as the day wore on, and he found that his reading was progressing well.
10150-8{_}476{_}He had worked out to a satisfactory conclusion all the problems which had as yet baffled him, and it was in a state of jubilation that he paid a visit to Mrs. Witham at 'The Good Traveller'.
10150-8{_}477{_}He found a stranger in the cosy sitting-room with the landlady, who was introduced to him as Dr. Thornhill.
10150-8{_}478{_}She was not quite at ease, and this, combined with the doctor's plunging at once into a series of questions, made Malcolmson come to the conclusion that his presence was not an accident, so without preliminary he said:  'Dr.
10150-8{_}479{_}Thornhill, I shall with pleasure answer you any question you may choose to ask me if you will answer me one question first.'
10150-8{_}480{_}The doctor seemed surprised, but he smiled and answered at once, 'Done!
10150-8{_}481{_}What is it?'
10150-8{_}482{_}'Did Mrs. Witham ask you to come here and see me and advise me?'
10150-8{_}483{_}Dr. Thornhill for a moment was taken aback, and Mrs. Witham got fiery red and turned away; but the doctor was a frank and ready man, and he answered at once and openly.
10150-8{_}484{_}'She did: but she didn't intend you to know it.
10150-8{_}485{_}I suppose it was my clumsy haste that made you suspect.
10150-8{_}486{_}She told me that she did not like the idea of your being in that house all by yourself, and that she thought you took too much strong tea.
10150-8{_}487{_}In fact, she wants me to advise you if possible to give up the tea and the very late hours.
10150-8{_}488{_}I was a keen student in my time, so I suppose I may take the liberty of a college man, and without offence, advise you not quite as a stranger.'
10150-8{_}489{_}Malcolmson with a bright smile held out his hand.
10150-8{_}490{_}'Shake!
10150-8{_}491{_}as they say in America,' he said.
10150-8{_}492{_}'I must thank you for your kindness and Mrs. Witham too, and your kindness deserves a return on my part.
10150-8{_}493{_}I promise to take no more strong tea--no tea at all till you let me--and I shall go to bed tonight at one o'clock at latest.
10150-8{_}494{_}Will that do?'
10150-8{_}495{_}'Capital,' said the doctor.
10150-8{_}496{_}'Now tell us all that you noticed in the old house,' and so Malcolmson then and there told in minute detail all that had happened in the last two nights.
10150-8{_}497{_}He was interrupted every now and then by some exclamation from Mrs. Witham, till finally when he told of the episode of the Bible the landlady's pent-up emotions found vent in a shriek; and it was not till a stiff glass of brandy and water had been administered that she grew composed again.
10150-8{_}498{_}Dr. Thornhill listened with a face of growing gravity, and when the narrative was complete and Mrs. Witham had been restored he asked:  'The rat always went up the rope of the alarm bell?'
10150-8{_}499{_}'Always.'
10150-8{_}500{_}'I suppose you know,' said the Doctor after a pause, 'what the rope is?'
10150-8{_}501{_}'No!'
10150-8{_}502{_}'It is,' said the Doctor slowly, 'the very rope which the hangman used for all the victims of the Judge's judicial rancour!'
10150-8{_}503{_}Here he was interrupted by another scream from Mrs. Witham, and steps had to be taken for her recovery.
10150-8{_}504{_}Malcolmson having looked at his watch, and found that it was close to his dinner hour, had gone home before her complete recovery.
10150-8{_}505{_}When Mrs. Witham was herself again she almost assailed the Doctor with angry questions as to what he meant by putting such horrible ideas into the poor young man's mind.
10150-8{_}506{_}'He has quite enough there already to upset him,' she added.
10150-8{_}507{_}Dr. Thornhill replied:  'My dear madam, I had a distinct purpose in it!
10150-8{_}508{_}I wanted to draw his attention to the bell rope, and to fix it there.
10150-8{_}509{_}It may be that he is in a highly overwrought state, and has been studying too much, although I am bound to say that he seems as sound and healthy a young man, mentally and bodily, as ever I saw--but then the rats--and that suggestion of the devil.'
10150-8{_}510{_}The doctor shook his head and went on.
10150-8{_}511{_}'I would have offered to go and stay the first night with him but that I felt sure it would have been a cause of offence.
10150-8{_}512{_}He may get in the night some strange fright or hallucination; and if he does I want him to pull that rope.
10150-8{_}513{_}All alone as he is it will give us warning, and we may reach him in time to be of service.
10150-8{_}514{_}I shall be sitting up pretty late tonight and shall keep my ears open.
10150-8{_}515{_}Do not be alarmed if Benchurch gets a surprise before morning.'
10150-8{_}516{_}'Oh, Doctor, what do you mean?
10150-8{_}517{_}What do you mean?'
10150-8{_}518{_}'I mean this; that possibly--nay, more probably--we shall hear the great alarm bell from the Judge's House tonight,' and the Doctor made about as effective an exit as could be thought of.
10150-8{_}519{_}When Malcolmson arrived home he found that it was a little after his usual time, and Mrs. Dempster had gone away--the rules of Greenhow's Charity were not to be neglected.
10150-8{_}520{_}He was glad to see that the place was bright and tidy with a cheerful fire and a well-trimmed lamp.
10150-8{_}521{_}The evening was colder than might have been expected in April, and a heavy wind was blowing with such rapidly-increasing strength that there was every promise of a storm during the night.
10150-8{_}522{_}For a few minutes after his entrance the noise of the rats ceased; but so soon as they became accustomed to his presence they began again.
10150-8{_}523{_}He was glad to hear them, for he felt once more the feeling of companionship in their noise, and his mind ran back to the strange fact that they only ceased to manifest themselves when that other--the great rat with the baleful eyes--came upon the scene.
10150-8{_}524{_}The reading-lamp only was lit and its green shade kept the ceiling and the upper part of the room in darkness, so that the cheerful light from the hearth spreading over the floor and shining on the white cloth laid over the end of the table was warm and cheery.
10150-8{_}525{_}Malcolmson sat down to his dinner with a good appetite and a buoyant spirit.
10150-8{_}526{_}After his dinner and a cigarette he sat steadily down to work, determined not to let anything disturb him, for he remembered his promise to the doctor, and made up his mind to make the best of the time at his disposal.
10150-8{_}527{_}For an hour or so he worked all right, and then his thoughts began to wander from his books.
10150-8{_}528{_}The actual circumstances around him, the calls on his physical attention, and his nervous susceptibility were not to be denied.
10150-8{_}529{_}By this time the wind had become a gale, and the gale a storm.
10150-8{_}530{_}The old house, solid though it was, seemed to shake to its foundations, and the storm roared and raged through its many chimneys and its queer old gables, producing strange, unearthly sounds in the empty rooms and corridors.
10150-8{_}531{_}Even the great alarm bell on the roof must have felt the force of the wind, for the rope rose and fell slightly, as though the bell were moved a little from time to time and the limber rope fell on the oak floor with a hard and hollow sound.
10150-8{_}532{_}As Malcolmson listened to it he bethought himself of the doctor's words, 'It is the rope which the hangman used for the victims of the Judge's judicial rancour,' and he went over to the corner of the fireplace and took it in his hand to look at it.
10150-8{_}533{_}There seemed a sort of deadly interest in it, and as he stood there he lost himself for a moment in speculation as to who these victims were, and the grim wish of the Judge to have such a ghastly relic ever under his eyes.
10150-8{_}534{_}As he stood there the swaying of the bell on the roof still lifted the rope now and again; but presently there came a new sensation--a sort of tremor in the rope, as though something was moving along it.
10150-8{_}535{_}Looking up instinctively Malcolmson saw the great rat coming slowly down towards him, glaring at him steadily.
10150-8{_}536{_}He dropped the rope and started back with a muttered curse, and the rat turning ran up the rope again and disappeared, and at the same instant Malcolmson became conscious that the noise of the rats, which had ceased for a while, began again.
10150-8{_}537{_}All this set him thinking, and it occurred to him that he had not investigated the lair of the rat or looked at the pictures, as he had intended.
10150-8{_}538{_}He lit the other lamp without the shade, and, holding it up went and stood opposite the third picture from the fireplace on the right-hand side where he had seen the rat disappear on the previous night.
10150-8{_}539{_}At the first glance he started back so suddenly that he almost dropped the lamp, and a deadly pallor overspread his face.
10150-8{_}540{_}His knees shook, and heavy drops of sweat came on his forehead, and he trembled like an aspen.
10150-8{_}541{_}But he was young and plucky, and pulled himself together, and after the pause of a few seconds stepped forward again, raised the lamp, and examined the picture which had been dusted and washed, and now stood out clearly.
10150-8{_}542{_}It was of a judge dressed in his robes of scarlet and ermine.
10150-8{_}543{_}His face was strong and merciless, evil, crafty, and vindictive, with a sensual mouth, hooked nose of ruddy colour, and shaped like the beak of a bird of prey.
10150-8{_}544{_}The rest of the face was of a cadaverous colour.
10150-8{_}545{_}The eyes were of peculiar brilliance and with a terribly malignant expression.
10150-8{_}546{_}As he looked at them, Malcolmson grew cold, for he saw there the very counterpart of the eyes of the great rat.
10150-8{_}547{_}The lamp almost fell from his hand, he saw the rat with its baleful eyes peering out through the hole in the corner of the picture, and noted the sudden cessation of the noise of the other rats.
10150-8{_}548{_}However, he pulled himself together, and went on with his examination of the picture.
10150-8{_}549{_}The Judge was seated in a great high-backed carved oak chair, on the right-hand side of a great stone fireplace where, in the corner, a rope hung down from the ceiling, its end lying coiled on the floor.
10150-8{_}550{_}With a feeling of something like horror, Malcolmson recognised the scene of the room as it stood, and gazed around him in an awestruck manner as though he expected to find some strange presence behind him.
10150-8{_}551{_}Then he looked over to the corner of the fireplace--and with a loud cry he let the lamp fall from his hand.
10150-8{_}552{_}There, in the Judge's arm-chair, with the rope hanging behind, sat the rat with the Judge's baleful eyes, now intensified and with a fiendish leer.
10150-8{_}553{_}Save for the howling of the storm without there was silence.
10150-8{_}554{_}The fallen lamp recalled Malcolmson to himself.
10150-8{_}555{_}Fortunately it was of metal, and so the oil was not spilt.
10150-8{_}556{_}However, the practical need of attending to it settled at once his nervous apprehensions.
10150-8{_}557{_}When he had turned it out, he wiped his brow and thought for a moment.
10150-8{_}558{_}'This will not do,' he said to himself.
10150-8{_}559{_}'If I go on like this I shall become a crazy fool.
10150-8{_}560{_}This must stop!
10150-8{_}561{_}I promised the doctor I would not take tea.
10150-8{_}562{_}Faith, he was pretty right!
10150-8{_}563{_}My nerves must have been getting into a queer state.
10150-8{_}564{_}Funny I did not notice it.
10150-8{_}565{_}I never felt better in my life.
10150-8{_}566{_}However, it is all right now, and I shall not be such a fool again.'
10150-8{_}567{_}Then he mixed himself a good stiff glass of brandy and water and resolutely sat down to his work.
10150-8{_}568{_}It was nearly an hour when he looked up from his book, disturbed by the sudden stillness.
10150-8{_}569{_}Without, the wind howled and roared louder than ever, and the rain drove in sheets against the windows, beating like hail on the glass; but within there was no sound whatever save the echo of the wind as it roared in the great chimney, and now and then a hiss as a few raindrops found their way down the chimney in a lull of the storm.
10150-8{_}570{_}The fire had fallen low and had ceased to flame, though it threw out a red glow.
10150-8{_}571{_}Malcolmson listened attentively, and presently heard a thin, squeaking noise, very faint.
10150-8{_}572{_}It came from the corner of the room where the rope hung down, and he thought it was the creaking of the rope on the floor as the swaying of the bell raised and lowered it.
10150-8{_}573{_}Looking up, however, he saw in the dim light the great rat clinging to the rope and gnawing it.
10150-8{_}574{_}The rope was already nearly gnawed through--he could see the lighter colour where the strands were laid bare.
10150-8{_}575{_}As he looked the job was completed, and the severed end of the rope fell clattering on the oaken floor, whilst for an instant the great rat remained like a knob or tassel at the end of the rope, which now began to sway to and fro.
10150-8{_}576{_}Malcolmson felt for a moment another pang of terror as he thought that now the possibility of calling the outer world to his assistance was cut off, but an intense anger took its place, and seizing the book he was reading he hurled it at the rat.
10150-8{_}577{_}The blow was well aimed, but before the missile could reach him the rat dropped off and struck the floor with a soft thud.
10150-8{_}578{_}Malcolmson instantly rushed over towards him, but it darted away and disappeared in the darkness of the shadows of the room.
10150-8{_}579{_}Malcolmson felt that his work was over for the night, and determined then and there to vary the monotony of the proceedings by a hunt for the rat, and took off the green shade of the lamp so as to insure a wider spreading light.
10150-8{_}580{_}As he did so the gloom of the upper part of the room was relieved, and in the new flood of light, great by comparison with the previous darkness, the pictures on the wall stood out boldly.
10150-8{_}581{_}From where he stood, Malcolmson saw right opposite to him the third picture on the wall from the right of the fireplace.
10150-8{_}582{_}He rubbed his eyes in surprise, and then a great fear began to come upon him.
10150-8{_}583{_}In the centre of the picture was a great irregular patch of brown canvas, as fresh as when it was stretched on the frame.
10150-8{_}584{_}The background was as before, with chair and chimney-corner and rope, but the figure of the Judge had disappeared.
10150-8{_}585{_}Malcolmson, almost in a chill of horror, turned slowly round, and then he began to shake and tremble like a man in a palsy.
10150-8{_}586{_}His strength seemed to have left him, and he was incapable of action or movement, hardly even of thought.
10150-8{_}587{_}He could only see and hear.
10150-8{_}588{_}There, on the great high-backed carved oak chair sat the Judge in his robes of scarlet and ermine, with his baleful eyes glaring vindictively, and a smile of triumph on the resolute, cruel mouth, as he lifted with his hands a _black cap_.
10150-8{_}589{_}Malcolmson felt as if the blood was running from his heart, as one does in moments of prolonged suspense.
10150-8{_}590{_}There was a singing in his ears.
10150-8{_}591{_}Without, he could hear the roar and howl of the tempest, and through it, swept on the storm, came the striking of midnight by the great chimes in the market place.
10150-8{_}592{_}He stood for a space of time that seemed to him endless still as a statue, and with wide-open, horror-struck eyes, breathless.
10150-8{_}593{_}As the clock struck, so the smile of triumph on the Judge's face intensified, and at the last stroke of midnight he placed the black cap on his head.
10150-8{_}594{_}Slowly and deliberately the Judge rose from his chair and picked up the piece of the rope of the alarm bell which lay on the floor, drew it through his hands as if he enjoyed its touch, and then deliberately began to knot one end of it, fashioning it into a noose.
10150-8{_}595{_}This he tightened and tested with his foot, pulling hard at it till he was satisfied and then making a running noose of it, which he held in his hand.
10150-8{_}596{_}Then he began to move along the table on the opposite side to Malcolmson keeping his eyes on him until he had passed him, when with a quick movement he stood in front of the door.
10150-8{_}597{_}Malcolmson then began to feel that he was trapped, and tried to think of what he should do.
10150-8{_}598{_}There was some fascination in the Judge's eyes, which he never took off him, and he had, perforce, to look.
10150-8{_}599{_}He saw the Judge approach--still keeping between him and the door--and raise the noose and throw it towards him as if to entangle him.
10150-8{_}600{_}With a great effort he made a quick movement to one side, and saw the rope fall beside him, and heard it strike the oaken floor.
10150-8{_}601{_}Again the Judge raised the noose and tried to ensnare him, ever keeping his baleful eyes fixed on him, and each time by a mighty effort the student just managed to evade it.
10150-8{_}602{_}So this went on for many times, the Judge seeming never discouraged nor discomposed at failure, but playing as a cat does with a mouse.
10150-8{_}603{_}At last in despair, which had reached its climax, Malcolmson cast a quick glance round him.
10150-8{_}604{_}The lamp seemed to have blazed up, and there was a fairly good light in the room.
10150-8{_}605{_}At the many rat-holes and in the chinks and crannies of the wainscot he saw the rats' eyes; and this aspect, that was purely physical, gave him a gleam of comfort.
10150-8{_}606{_}He looked around and saw that the rope of the great alarm bell was laden with rats.
10150-8{_}607{_}Every inch of it was covered with them, and more and more were pouring through the small circular hole in the ceiling whence it emerged, so that with their weight the bell was beginning to sway.
10150-8{_}608{_}Hark!
10150-8{_}609{_}it had swayed till the clapper had touched the bell.
10150-8{_}610{_}The sound was but a tiny one, but the bell was only beginning to sway, and it would increase.
10150-8{_}611{_}At the sound the Judge, who had been keeping his eyes fixed on Malcolmson, looked up, and a scowl of diabolical anger overspread his face.
10150-8{_}612{_}His eyes fairly glowed like hot coals, and he stamped his foot with a sound that seemed to make the house shake.
10150-8{_}613{_}A dreadful peal of thunder broke overhead as he raised the rope again, whilst the rats kept running up and down the rope as though working against time.
10150-8{_}614{_}This time, instead of throwing it, he drew close to his victim, and held open the noose as he approached.
10150-8{_}615{_}As he came closer there seemed something paralysing in his very presence, and Malcolmson stood rigid as a corpse.
10150-8{_}616{_}He felt the Judge's icy fingers touch his throat as he adjusted the rope.
10150-8{_}617{_}The noose tightened--tightened.
10150-8{_}618{_}Then the Judge, taking the rigid form of the student in his arms, carried him over and placed him standing in the oak chair, and stepping up beside him, put his hand up and caught the end of the swaying rope of the alarm bell.
10150-8{_}619{_}As he raised his hand the rats fled squeaking, and disappeared through the hole in the ceiling.
10150-8{_}620{_}Taking the end of the noose which was round Malcolmson's neck he tied it to the hanging-bell rope, and then descending pulled away the chair.
10150-8{_}621{_}*       *       *       *       *  When the alarm bell of the Judge's House began to sound a crowd soon assembled.
10150-8{_}622{_}Lights and torches of various kinds appeared, and soon a silent crowd was hurrying to the spot.
10150-8{_}623{_}They knocked loudly at the door, but there was no reply.
10150-8{_}624{_}Then they burst in the door, and poured into the great dining-room, the doctor at the head.
10150-8{_}625{_}There at the end of the rope of the great alarm bell hung the body of the student, and on the face of the Judge in the picture was a malignant smile.
10150-8{_}626{_}The Squaw   Nurnberg at the time was not so much exploited as it has been since then.
10150-8{_}627{_}Irving had not been playing _Faust_, and the very name of the old town was hardly known to the great bulk of the travelling public.
10150-8{_}628{_}My wife and I being in the second week of our honeymoon, naturally wanted someone else to join our party, so that when the cheery stranger, Elias P. Hutcheson, hailing from Isthmian City, Bleeding Gulch, Maple Tree County, Neb.
