10150-8{_}2017{_}My pursuers were still well behind me, but struggling up resolutely, and in deadly silence.
10150-8{_}2018{_}Beyond, the shanty was a wreck--a mass of timber and moving forms.
10150-8{_}2019{_}I could see it well, for flames were already bursting out; the rags and straw had evidently caught fire from the lantern.
10150-8{_}2020{_}Still silence there!
10150-8{_}2021{_}Not a sound!
10150-8{_}2022{_}These old wretches could die game, anyhow.
10150-8{_}2023{_}I had no time for more than a passing glance, for as I cast an eye round the mound preparatory to making my descent I saw several dark forms rushing round on either side to cut me off on my way.
10150-8{_}2024{_}It was now a race for life.
10150-8{_}2025{_}They were trying to head me on my way to Paris, and with the instinct of the moment I dashed down to the right-hand side.
10150-8{_}2026{_}I was just in time, for, though I came as it seemed to me down the steep in a few steps, the wary old men who were watching me turned back, and one, as I rushed by into the opening between the two mounds in front, almost struck me a blow with that terrible butcher's axe.
10150-8{_}2027{_}There could surely not be two such weapons about!
10150-8{_}2028{_}Then began a really horrible chase.
10150-8{_}2029{_}I easily ran ahead of the old men, and even when some younger ones and a few women joined in the hunt I easily distanced them.
10150-8{_}2030{_}But I did not know the way, and I could not even guide myself by the light in the sky, for I was running away from it.
10150-8{_}2031{_}I had heard that, unless of conscious purpose, hunted men turn always to the left, and so I found it now; and so, I suppose, knew also my pursuers, who were more animals than men, and with cunning or instinct had found out such secrets for themselves: for on finishing a quick spurt, after which I intended to take a moment's breathing space, I suddenly saw ahead of me two or three forms swiftly passing behind a mound to the right.
10150-8{_}2032{_}I was in the spider's web now indeed!
10150-8{_}2033{_}But with the thought of this new danger came the resource of the hunted, and so I darted down the next turning to the right.
10150-8{_}2034{_}I continued in this direction for some hundred yards, and then, making a turn to the left again, felt certain that I had, at any rate, avoided the danger of being surrounded.
10150-8{_}2035{_}But not of pursuit, for on came the rabble after me, steady, dogged, relentless, and still in grim silence.
10150-8{_}2036{_}In the greater darkness the mounds seemed now to be somewhat smaller than before, although--for the night was closing--they looked bigger in proportion.
10150-8{_}2037{_}I was now well ahead of my pursuers, so I made a dart up the mound in front.
10150-8{_}2038{_}Oh joy of joys!
10150-8{_}2039{_}I was close to the edge of this inferno of dustheaps.
10150-8{_}2040{_}Away behind me the red light of Paris was in the sky, and towering up behind rose the heights of Montmarte--a dim light, with here and there brilliant points like stars.
10150-8{_}2041{_}Restored to vigour in a moment, I ran over the few remaining mounds of decreasing size, and found myself on the level land beyond.
10150-8{_}2042{_}Even then, however, the prospect was not inviting.
10150-8{_}2043{_}All before me was dark and dismal, and I had evidently come on one of those dank, low-lying waste places which are found here and there in the neighbourhood of great cities.
10150-8{_}2044{_}Places of waste and desolation, where the space is required for the ultimate agglomeration of all that is noxious, and the ground is so poor as to create no desire of occupancy even in the lowest squatter.
10150-8{_}2045{_}With eyes accustomed to the gloom of the evening, and away now from the shadows of those dreadful dustheaps, I could see much more easily than I could a little while ago.
10150-8{_}2046{_}It might have been, of course, that the glare in the sky of the lights of Paris, though the city was some miles away, was reflected here.
10150-8{_}2047{_}Howsoever it was, I saw well enough to take bearings for certainly some little distance around me.
10150-8{_}2048{_}In front was a bleak, flat waste that seemed almost dead level, with here and there the dark shimmering of stagnant pools.
10150-8{_}2049{_}Seemingly far off on the right, amid a small cluster of scattered lights, rose a dark mass of Fort Montrouge, and away to the left in the dim distance, pointed with stray gleams from cottage windows, the lights in the sky showed the locality of Bicêtre.
10150-8{_}2050{_}A moment's thought decided me to take to the right and try to reach Montrouge.
10150-8{_}2051{_}There at least would be some sort of safety, and I might possibly long before come on some of the cross roads which I knew.
10150-8{_}2052{_}Somewhere, not far off, must lie the strategic road made to connect the outlying chain of forts circling the city.
10150-8{_}2053{_}Then I looked back.
10150-8{_}2054{_}Coming over the mounds, and outlined black against the glare of the Parisian horizon, I saw several moving figures, and still a way to the right several more deploying out between me and my destination.
10150-8{_}2055{_}They evidently meant to cut me off in this direction, and so my choice became constricted; it lay now between going straight ahead or turning to the left.
10150-8{_}2056{_}Stooping to the ground, so as to get the advantage of the horizon as a line of sight, I looked carefully in this direction, but could detect no sign of my enemies.
10150-8{_}2057{_}I argued that as they had not guarded or were not trying to guard that point, there was evidently danger to me there already.
10150-8{_}2058{_}So I made up my mind to go straight on before me.
10150-8{_}2059{_}It was not an inviting prospect, and as I went on the reality grew worse.
10150-8{_}2060{_}The ground became soft and oozy, and now and again gave way beneath me in a sickening kind of way.
10150-8{_}2061{_}I seemed somehow to be going down, for I saw round me places seemingly more elevated than where I was, and this in a place which from a little way back seemed dead level.
10150-8{_}2062{_}I looked around, but could see none of my pursuers.
10150-8{_}2063{_}This was strange, for all along these birds of the night had followed me through the darkness as well as though it was broad daylight.
10150-8{_}2064{_}How I blamed myself for coming out in my light-coloured tourist suit of tweed.
10150-8{_}2065{_}The silence, and my not being able to see my enemies, whilst I felt that they were watching me, grew appalling, and in the hope of some one not of this ghastly crew hearing me I raised my voice and shouted several times.
10150-8{_}2066{_}There was not the slightest response; not even an echo rewarded my efforts.
10150-8{_}2067{_}For a while I stood stock still and kept my eyes in one direction.
10150-8{_}2068{_}On one of the rising places around me I saw something dark move along, then another, and another.
10150-8{_}2069{_}This was to my left, and seemingly moving to head me off.
10150-8{_}2070{_}I thought that again I might with my skill as a runner elude my enemies at this game, and so with all my speed darted forward.
10150-8{_}2071{_}Splash!
10150-8{_}2072{_}My feet had given way in a mass of slimy rubbish, and I had fallen headlong into a reeking, stagnant pool.
10150-8{_}2073{_}The water and the mud in which my arms sank up to the elbows was filthy and nauseous beyond description, and in the suddenness of my fall I had actually swallowed some of the filthy stuff, which nearly choked me, and made me gasp for breath.
10150-8{_}2074{_}Never shall I forget the moments during which I stood trying to recover myself almost fainting from the foetid odour of the filthy pool, whose white mist rose ghostlike around.
10150-8{_}2075{_}Worst of all, with the acute despair of the hunted animal when he sees the pursuing pack closing on him, I saw before my eyes whilst I stood helpless the dark forms of my pursuers moving swiftly to surround me.
10150-8{_}2076{_}It is curious how our minds work on odd matters even when the energies of thought are seemingly concentrated on some terrible and pressing need.
10150-8{_}2077{_}I was in momentary peril of my life: my safety depended on my action, and my choice of alternatives coming now with almost every step I took, and yet I could not but think of the strange dogged persistency of these old men.
10150-8{_}2078{_}Their silent resolution, their steadfast, grim, persistency even in such a cause commanded, as well as fear, even a measure of respect.
10150-8{_}2079{_}What must they have been in the vigour of their youth.
10150-8{_}2080{_}I could understand now that whirlwind rush on the bridge of Arcola, that scornful exclamation of the Old Guard at Waterloo!
10150-8{_}2081{_}Unconscious cerebration has its own pleasures, even at such moments; but fortunately it does not in any way clash with the thought from which action springs.
10150-8{_}2082{_}I realised at a glance that so far I was defeated in my object, my enemies as yet had won.
10150-8{_}2083{_}They had succeeded in surrounding me on three sides, and were bent on driving me off to the left-hand, where there was already some danger for me, for they had left no guard.
10150-8{_}2084{_}I accepted the alternative--it was a case of Hobson's choice and run.
10150-8{_}2085{_}I had to keep the lower ground, for my pursuers were on the higher places.
10150-8{_}2086{_}However, though the ooze and broken ground impeded me my youth and training made me able to hold my ground, and by keeping a diagonal line I not only kept them from gaining on me but even began to distance them.
10150-8{_}2087{_}This gave me new heart and strength, and by this time habitual training was beginning to tell and my second wind had come.
10150-8{_}2088{_}Before me the ground rose slightly.
10150-8{_}2089{_}I rushed up the slope and found before me a waste of watery slime, with a low dyke or bank looking black and grim beyond.
10150-8{_}2090{_}I felt that if I could but reach that dyke in safety I could there, with solid ground under my feet and some kind of path to guide me, find with comparative ease a way out of my troubles.
10150-8{_}2091{_}After a glance right and left and seeing no one near, I kept my eyes for a few minutes to their rightful work of aiding my feet whilst I crossed the swamp.
10150-8{_}2092{_}It was rough, hard work, but there was little danger, merely toil; and a short time took me to the dyke.
10150-8{_}2093{_}I rushed up the slope exulting; but here again I met a new shock.
10150-8{_}2094{_}On either side of me rose a number of crouching figures.
10150-8{_}2095{_}From right and left they rushed at me.
10150-8{_}2096{_}Each body held a rope.
10150-8{_}2097{_}The cordon was nearly complete.
10150-8{_}2098{_}I could pass on neither side, and the end was near.
10150-8{_}2099{_}There was only one chance, and I took it.
10150-8{_}2100{_}I hurled myself across the dyke, and escaping out of the very clutches of my foes threw myself into the stream.
10150-8{_}2101{_}At any other time I should have thought that water foul and filthy, but now it was as welcome as the most crystal stream to the parched traveller.
10150-8{_}2102{_}It was a highway of safety!
10150-8{_}2103{_}My pursuers rushed after me.
10150-8{_}2104{_}Had only one of them held the rope it would have been all up with me, for he could have entangled me before I had time to swim a stroke; but the many hands holding it embarrassed and delayed them, and when the rope struck the water I heard the splash well behind me.
10150-8{_}2105{_}A few minutes' hard swimming took me across the stream.
10150-8{_}2106{_}Refreshed with the immersion and encouraged by the escape, I climbed the dyke in comparative gaiety of spirits.
10150-8{_}2107{_}From the top I looked back.
10150-8{_}2108{_}Through the darkness I saw my assailants scattering up and down along the dyke.
10150-8{_}2109{_}The pursuit was evidently not ended, and again I had to choose my course.
10150-8{_}2110{_}Beyond the dyke where I stood was a wild, swampy space very similar to that which I had crossed.
10150-8{_}2111{_}I determined to shun such a place, and thought for a moment whether I would take up or down the dyke.
10150-8{_}2112{_}I thought I heard a sound--the muffled sound of oars, so I listened, and then shouted.
10150-8{_}2113{_}No response; but the sound ceased.
10150-8{_}2114{_}My enemies had evidently got a boat of some kind.
10150-8{_}2115{_}As they were on the up side of me I took the down path and began to run.
10150-8{_}2116{_}As I passed to the left of where I had entered the water I heard several splashes, soft and stealthy, like the sound a rat makes as he plunges into the stream, but vastly greater; and as I looked I saw the dark sheen of the water broken by the ripples of several advancing heads.
10150-8{_}2117{_}Some of my enemies were swimming the stream also.
10150-8{_}2118{_}And now behind me, up the stream, the silence was broken by the quick rattle and creak of oars; my enemies were in hot pursuit.
10150-8{_}2119{_}I put my best leg foremost and ran on.
10150-8{_}2120{_}After a break of a couple of minutes I looked back, and by a gleam of light through the ragged clouds I saw several dark forms climbing the bank behind me.
10150-8{_}2121{_}The wind had now begun to rise, and the water beside me was ruffled and beginning to break in tiny waves on the bank.
10150-8{_}2122{_}I had to keep my eyes pretty well on the ground before me, lest I should stumble, for I knew that to stumble was death.
10150-8{_}2123{_}After a few minutes I looked back behind me.
10150-8{_}2124{_}On the dyke were only a few dark figures, but crossing the waste, swampy ground were many more.
10150-8{_}2125{_}What new danger this portended I did not know--could only guess.
10150-8{_}2126{_}Then as I ran it seemed to me that my track kept ever sloping away to the right.
10150-8{_}2127{_}I looked up ahead and saw that the river was much wider than before, and that the dyke on which I stood fell quite away, and beyond it was another stream on whose near bank I saw some of the dark forms now across the marsh.
10150-8{_}2128{_}I was on an island of some kind.
10150-8{_}2129{_}My situation was now indeed terrible, for my enemies had hemmed me in on every side.
10150-8{_}2130{_}Behind came the quickening roll of the oars, as though my pursuers knew that the end was close.
10150-8{_}2131{_}Around me on every side was desolation; there was not a roof or light, as far as I could see.
10150-8{_}2132{_}Far off to the right rose some dark mass, but what it was I knew not.
10150-8{_}2133{_}For a moment I paused to think what I should do, not for more, for my pursuers were drawing closer.
10150-8{_}2134{_}Then my mind was made up.
10150-8{_}2135{_}I slipped down the bank and took to the water.
10150-8{_}2136{_}I struck out straight ahead so as to gain the current by clearing the backwater of the island, for such I presume it was, when I had passed into the stream.
10150-8{_}2137{_}I waited till a cloud came driving across the moon and leaving all in darkness.
10150-8{_}2138{_}Then I took off my hat and laid it softly on the water floating with the stream, and a second after dived to the right and struck out under water with all my might.
10150-8{_}2139{_}I was, I suppose, half a minute under water, and when I rose came up as softly as I could, and turning, looked back.
10150-8{_}2140{_}There went my light brown hat floating merrily away.
10150-8{_}2141{_}Close behind it came a rickety old boat, driven furiously by a pair of oars.
10150-8{_}2142{_}The moon was still partly obscured by the drifting clouds, but in the partial light I could see a man in the bows holding aloft ready to strike what appeared to me to be that same dreadful pole-axe which I had before escaped.
10150-8{_}2143{_}As I looked the boat drew closer, closer, and the man struck savagely.
10150-8{_}2144{_}The hat disappeared.
10150-8{_}2145{_}The man fell forward, almost out of the boat.
10150-8{_}2146{_}His comrades dragged him in but without the axe, and then as I turned with all my energies bent on reaching the further bank, I heard the fierce whirr of the muttered 'Sacre!'
10150-8{_}2147{_}which marked the anger of my baffled pursuers.
10150-8{_}2148{_}That was the first sound I had heard from human lips during all this dreadful chase, and full as it was of menace and danger to me it was a welcome sound for it broke that awful silence which shrouded and appalled me.
10150-8{_}2149{_}It was as though an overt sign that my opponents were men and not ghosts, and that with them I had, at least, the chance of a man, though but one against many.
10150-8{_}2150{_}But now that the spell of silence was broken the sounds came thick and fast.
10150-8{_}2151{_}From boat to shore and back from shore to boat came quick question and answer, all in the fiercest whispers.
10150-8{_}2152{_}I looked back--a fatal thing to do--for in the instant someone caught sight of my face, which showed white on the dark water, and shouted.
10150-8{_}2153{_}Hands pointed to me, and in a moment or two the boat was under weigh, and following hard after me.
10150-8{_}2154{_}I had but a little way to go, but quicker and quicker came the boat after me.
10150-8{_}2155{_}A few more strokes and I would be on the shore, but I felt the oncoming of the boat, and expected each second to feel the crash of an oar or other weapon on my head.
10150-8{_}2156{_}Had I not seen that dreadful axe disappear in the water I do not think that I could have won the shore.
10150-8{_}2157{_}I heard the muttered curses of those not rowing and the laboured breath of the rowers.
10150-8{_}2158{_}With one supreme effort for life or liberty I touched the bank and sprang up it.
10150-8{_}2159{_}There was not a single second to spare, for hard behind me the boat grounded and several dark forms sprang after me.
10150-8{_}2160{_}I gained the top of the dyke, and keeping to the left ran on again.
10150-8{_}2161{_}The boat put off and followed down the stream.
10150-8{_}2162{_}Seeing this I feared danger in this direction, and quickly turning, ran down the dyke on the other side, and after passing a short stretch of marshy ground gained a wild, open flat country and sped on.
10150-8{_}2163{_}Still behind me came on my relentless pursuers.
10150-8{_}2164{_}Far away, below me, I saw the same dark mass as before, but now grown closer and greater.
10150-8{_}2165{_}My heart gave a great thrill of delight, for I knew that it must be the fortress of Bicêtre, and with new courage I ran on.
10150-8{_}2166{_}I had heard that between each and all of the protecting forts of Paris there are strategic ways, deep sunk roads where soldiers marching should be sheltered from an enemy.
10150-8{_}2167{_}I knew that if I could gain this road I would be safe, but in the darkness I could not see any sign of it, so, in blind hope of striking it, I ran on.
10150-8{_}2168{_}Presently I came to the edge of a deep cut, and found that down below me ran a road guarded on each side by a ditch of water fenced on either side by a straight, high wall.
10150-8{_}2169{_}Getting fainter and dizzier, I ran on; the ground got more broken--more and more still, till I staggered and fell, and rose again, and ran on in the blind anguish of the hunted.
10150-8{_}2170{_}Again the thought of Alice nerved me.
10150-8{_}2171{_}I would not be lost and wreck her life: I would fight and struggle for life to the bitter end.
10150-8{_}2172{_}With a great effort I caught the top of the wall.
10150-8{_}2173{_}As, scrambling like a catamount, I drew myself up, I actually felt a hand touch the sole of my foot.
10150-8{_}2174{_}I was now on a sort of causeway, and before me I saw a dim light.
10150-8{_}2175{_}Blind and dizzy, I ran on, staggered, and fell, rising, covered with dust and blood.
10150-8{_}2176{_}'Halt la!'
10150-8{_}2177{_}The words sounded like a voice from heaven.
10150-8{_}2178{_}A blaze of light seemed to enwrap me, and I shouted with joy.
10150-8{_}2179{_}'Qui va la?'
10150-8{_}2180{_}The rattle of musketry, the flash of steel before my eyes.
10150-8{_}2181{_}Instinctively I stopped, though close behind me came a rush of my pursuers.
10150-8{_}2182{_}Another word or two, and out from a gateway poured, as it seemed to me, a tide of red and blue, as the guard turned out.
10150-8{_}2183{_}All around seemed blazing with light, and the flash of steel, the clink and rattle of arms, and the loud, harsh voices of command.
10150-8{_}2184{_}As I fell forward, utterly exhausted, a soldier caught me.
10150-8{_}2185{_}I looked back in dreadful expectation, and saw the mass of dark forms disappearing into the night.
10150-8{_}2186{_}Then I must have fainted.
10150-8{_}2187{_}When I recovered my senses I was in the guard room.
10150-8{_}2188{_}They gave me brandy, and after a while I was able to tell them something of what had passed.
10150-8{_}2189{_}Then a commissary of police appeared, apparently out of the empty air, as is the way of the Parisian police officer.
10150-8{_}2190{_}He listened attentively, and then had a moment's consultation with the officer in command.
10150-8{_}2191{_}Apparently they were agreed, for they asked me if I were ready now to come with them.
10150-8{_}2192{_}'Where to?'
10150-8{_}2193{_}I asked, rising to go.
10150-8{_}2194{_}'Back to the dust heaps.
10150-8{_}2195{_}We shall, perhaps, catch them yet!'
10150-8{_}2196{_}'I shall try!'
10150-8{_}2197{_}said I.
10150-8{_}2198{_}He eyed me for a moment keenly, and said suddenly:  'Would you like to wait a while or till tomorrow, young Englishman?'
10150-8{_}2199{_}This touched me to the quick, as, perhaps, he intended, and I jumped to my feet.
10150-8{_}2200{_}'Come now!'
10150-8{_}2201{_}I said; 'now!
10150-8{_}2202{_}now!
10150-8{_}2203{_}An Englishman is always ready for his duty!'
10150-8{_}2204{_}The commissary was a good fellow, as well as a shrewd one; he slapped my shoulder kindly.
10150-8{_}2205{_}'Brave garçon!'
10150-8{_}2206{_}he said.
10150-8{_}2207{_}'Forgive me, but I knew what would do you most good.
10150-8{_}2208{_}The guard is ready.
10150-8{_}2209{_}Come!'
10150-8{_}2210{_}And so, passing right through the guard room, and through a long vaulted passage, we were out into the night.
10150-8{_}2211{_}A few of the men in front had powerful lanterns.
10150-8{_}2212{_}Through courtyards and down a sloping way we passed out through a low archway to a sunken road, the same that I had seen in my flight.
10150-8{_}2213{_}The order was given to get at the double, and with a quick, springing stride, half run, half walk, the soldiers went swiftly along.
10150-8{_}2214{_}I felt my strength renewed again--such is the difference between hunter and hunted.
10150-8{_}2215{_}A very short distance took us to a low-lying pontoon bridge across the stream, and evidently very little higher up than I had struck it.
10150-8{_}2216{_}Some effort had evidently been made to damage it, for the ropes had all been cut, and one of the chains had been broken.
10150-8{_}2217{_}I heard the officer say to the commissary:  'We are just in time!
10150-8{_}2218{_}A few more minutes, and they would have destroyed the bridge.
10150-8{_}2219{_}Forward, quicker still!'
10150-8{_}2220{_}and on we went.
10150-8{_}2221{_}Again we reached a pontoon on the winding stream; as we came up we heard the hollow boom of the metal drums as the efforts to destroy the bridge was again renewed.
10150-8{_}2222{_}A word of command was given, and several men raised their rifles.
10150-8{_}2223{_}'Fire!'
10150-8{_}2224{_}A volley rang out.
10150-8{_}2225{_}There was a muffled cry, and the dark forms dispersed.
10150-8{_}2226{_}But the evil was done, and we saw the far end of the pontoon swing into the stream.
10150-8{_}2227{_}This was a serious delay, and it was nearly an hour before we had renewed ropes and restored the bridge sufficiently to allow us to cross.
10150-8{_}2228{_}We renewed the chase.
10150-8{_}2229{_}Quicker, quicker we went towards the dust heaps.
10150-8{_}2230{_}After a time we came to a place that I knew.
10150-8{_}2231{_}There were the remains of a fire--a few smouldering wood ashes still cast a red glow, but the bulk of the ashes were cold.
10150-8{_}2232{_}I knew the site of the hut and the hill behind it up which I had rushed, and in the flickering glow the eyes of the rats still shone with a sort of phosphorescence.
10150-8{_}2233{_}The commissary spoke a word to the officer, and he cried:  'Halt!'
10150-8{_}2234{_}The soldiers were ordered to spread around and watch, and then we commenced to examine the ruins.
10150-8{_}2235{_}The commissary himself began to lift away the charred boards and rubbish.
10150-8{_}2236{_}These the soldiers took and piled together.
10150-8{_}2237{_}Presently he started back, then bent down and rising beckoned me.
10150-8{_}2238{_}'See!'
10150-8{_}2239{_}he said.
10150-8{_}2240{_}It was a gruesome sight.
10150-8{_}2241{_}There lay a skeleton face downwards, a woman by the lines--an old woman by the coarse fibre of the bone.
10150-8{_}2242{_}Between the ribs rose a long spike-like dagger made from a butcher's sharpening knife, its keen point buried in the spine.
10150-8{_}2243{_}'You will observe,' said the commissary to the officer and to me as he took out his note book, 'that the woman must have fallen on her dagger.
10150-8{_}2244{_}The rats are many here--see their eyes glistening among that heap of bones--and you will also notice'--I shuddered as he placed his hand on the skeleton--'that but little time was lost by them, for the bones are scarcely cold!'
10150-8{_}2245{_}There was no other sign of any one near, living or dead; and so deploying again into line the soldiers passed on.
10150-8{_}2246{_}Presently we came to the hut made of the old wardrobe.
10150-8{_}2247{_}We approached.
10150-8{_}2248{_}In five of the six compartments was an old man sleeping--sleeping so soundly that even the glare of the lanterns did not wake them.
10150-8{_}2249{_}Old and grim and grizzled they looked, with their gaunt, wrinkled, bronzed faces and their white moustaches.
10150-8{_}2250{_}The officer called out harshly and loudly a word of command, and in an instant each one of them was on his feet before us and standing at 'attention!'
10150-8{_}2251{_}'What do you here?'
10150-8{_}2252{_}'We sleep,' was the answer.
10150-8{_}2253{_}'Where are the other chiffoniers?'
10150-8{_}2254{_}asked the commissary.
10150-8{_}2255{_}'Gone to work.'
10150-8{_}2256{_}'And you?'
10150-8{_}2257{_}'We are on guard!'
10150-8{_}2258{_}'Peste!'
10150-8{_}2259{_}laughed the officer grimly, as he looked at the old men one after the other in the face and added with cool deliberate cruelty: 'Asleep on duty!
10150-8{_}2260{_}Is this the manner of the Old Guard?
10150-8{_}2261{_}No wonder, then, a Waterloo!'
10150-8{_}2262{_}By the gleam of the lantern I saw the grim old faces grow deadly pale, and almost shuddered at the look in the eyes of the old men as the laugh of the soldiers echoed the grim pleasantry of the officer.
10150-8{_}2263{_}I felt in that moment that I was in some measure avenged.
10150-8{_}2264{_}For a moment they looked as if they would throw themselves on the taunter, but years of their life had schooled them and they remained still.
10150-8{_}2265{_}'You are but five,' said the commissary; 'where is the sixth?'
10150-8{_}2266{_}The answer came with a grim chuckle.
10150-8{_}2267{_}'He is there!'
10150-8{_}2268{_}and the speaker pointed to the bottom of the wardrobe.
10150-8{_}2269{_}'He died last night.
10150-8{_}2270{_}You won't find much of him.
10150-8{_}2271{_}The burial of the rats is quick!'
10150-8{_}2272{_}The commissary stooped and looked in.
10150-8{_}2273{_}Then he turned to the officer and said calmly:  'We may as well go back.
10150-8{_}2274{_}No trace here now; nothing to prove that man was the one wounded by your soldiers' bullets!
10150-8{_}2275{_}Probably they murdered him to cover up the trace.
10150-8{_}2276{_}See!'
10150-8{_}2277{_}again he stooped and placed his hands on the skeleton.
10150-8{_}2278{_}'The rats work quickly and they are many.
10150-8{_}2279{_}These bones are warm!'
10150-8{_}2280{_}I shuddered, and so did many more of those around me.
10150-8{_}2281{_}'Form!'
10150-8{_}2282{_}said the officer, and so in marching order, with the lanterns swinging in front and the manacled veterans in the midst, with steady tramp we took ourselves out of the dustheaps and turned backward to the fortress of Bicêtre.
10150-8{_}2283{_}*       *       *       *       *  My year of probation has long since ended, and Alice is my wife.
10150-8{_}2284{_}But when I look back upon that trying twelvemonth one of the most vivid incidents that memory recalls is that associated with my visit to the City of Dust.
10150-8{_}2285{_}A Dream of Red Hands   The first opinion given to me regarding Jacob Settle was a simple descriptive statement, 'He's a down-in-the-mouth chap': but I found that it embodied the thoughts and ideas of all his fellow-workmen.
10150-8{_}2286{_}There was in the phrase a certain easy tolerance, an absence of positive feeling of any kind, rather than any complete opinion, which marked pretty accurately the man's place in public esteem.
10150-8{_}2287{_}Still, there was some dissimilarity between this and his appearance which unconsciously set me thinking, and by degrees, as I saw more of the place and the workmen, I came to have a special interest in him.
10150-8{_}2288{_}He was, I found, for ever doing kindnesses, not involving money expenses beyond his humble means, but in the manifold ways of forethought and forbearance and self-repression which are of the truer charities of life.
10150-8{_}2289{_}Women and children trusted him implicitly, though, strangely enough, he rather shunned them, except when anyone was sick, and then he made his appearance to help if he could, timidly and awkwardly.
10150-8{_}2290{_}He led a very solitary life, keeping house by himself in a tiny cottage, or rather hut, of one room, far on the edge of the moorland.
10150-8{_}2291{_}His existence seemed so sad and solitary that I wished to cheer it up, and for the purpose took the occasion when we had both been sitting up with a child, injured by me through accident, to offer to lend him books.
10150-8{_}2292{_}He gladly accepted, and as we parted in the grey of the dawn I felt that something of mutual confidence had been established between us.
10150-8{_}2293{_}The books were always most carefully and punctually returned, and in time Jacob Settle and I became quite friends.
10150-8{_}2294{_}Once or twice as I crossed the moorland on Sundays I looked in on him; but on such occasions he was shy and ill at ease so that I felt diffident about calling to see him.
10150-8{_}2295{_}He would never under any circumstances come into my own lodgings.
10150-8{_}2296{_}One Sunday afternoon, I was coming back from a long walk beyond the moor, and as I passed Settle's cottage stopped at the door to say 'How do you do?'
10150-8{_}2297{_}to him.
10150-8{_}2298{_}As the door was shut, I thought that he was out, and merely knocked for form's sake, or through habit, not expecting to get any answer.
10150-8{_}2299{_}To my surprise, I heard a feeble voice from within, though what was said I could not hear.
10150-8{_}2300{_}I entered at once, and found Jacob lying half-dressed upon his bed.
10150-8{_}2301{_}He was as pale as death, and the sweat was simply rolling off his face.
10150-8{_}2302{_}His hands were unconsciously gripping the bedclothes as a drowning man holds on to whatever he may grasp.
10150-8{_}2303{_}As I came in he half arose, with a wild, hunted look in his eyes, which were wide open and staring, as though something of horror had come before him; but when he recognised me he sank back on the couch with a smothered sob of relief and closed his eyes.
10150-8{_}2304{_}I stood by him for a while, quite a minute or two, while he gasped.
10150-8{_}2305{_}Then he opened his eyes and looked at me, but with such a despairing, woeful expression that, as I am a living man, I would have rather seen that frozen look of horror.
10150-8{_}2306{_}I sat down beside him and asked after his health.
10150-8{_}2307{_}For a while he would not answer me except to say that he was not ill; but then, after scrutinising me closely, he half arose on his elbow and said:  'I thank you kindly, sir, but I'm simply telling you the truth.
10150-8{_}2308{_}I am not ill, as men call it, though God knows whether there be not worse sicknesses than doctors know of.
10150-8{_}2309{_}I'll tell you, as you are so kind, but I trust that you won't even mention such a thing to a living soul, for it might work me more and greater woe.
10150-8{_}2310{_}I am suffering from a bad dream.'
10150-8{_}2311{_}'A bad dream!'
10150-8{_}2312{_}I said, hoping to cheer him; 'but dreams pass away with the light--even with waking.'
10150-8{_}2313{_}There I stopped, for before he spoke I saw the answer in his desolate look round the little place.
10150-8{_}2314{_}'No!
10150-8{_}2315{_}no!
10150-8{_}2316{_}that's all well for people that live in comfort and with those they love around them.
10150-8{_}2317{_}It is a thousand times worse for those who live alone and have to do so.
10150-8{_}2318{_}What cheer is there for me, waking here in the silence of the night, with the wide moor around me full of voices and full of faces that make my waking a worse dream than my sleep?
10150-8{_}2319{_}Ah, young sir, you have no past that can send its legions to people the darkness and the empty space, and I pray the good God that you may never have!'
10150-8{_}2320{_}As he spoke, there was such an almost irresistible gravity of conviction in his manner that I abandoned my remonstrance about his solitary life.
10150-8{_}2321{_}I felt that I was in the presence of some secret influence which I could not fathom.
10150-8{_}2322{_}To my relief, for I knew not what to say, he went on:  'Two nights past have I dreamed it.
10150-8{_}2323{_}It was hard enough the first night, but I came through it.
10150-8{_}2324{_}Last night the expectation was in itself almost worse than the dream--until the dream came, and then it swept away every remembrance of lesser pain.
10150-8{_}2325{_}I stayed awake till just before the dawn, and then it came again, and ever since I have been in such an agony as I am sure the dying feel, and with it all the dread of tonight.'
10150-8{_}2326{_}Before he had got to the end of the sentence my mind was made up, and I felt that I could speak to him more cheerfully.
10150-8{_}2327{_}'Try and get to sleep early tonight--in fact, before the evening has passed away.
10150-8{_}2328{_}The sleep will refresh you, and I promise you there will not be any bad dreams after tonight.'
10150-8{_}2329{_}He shook his head hopelessly, so I sat a little longer and then left him.
10150-8{_}2330{_}When I got home I made my arrangements for the night, for I had made up my mind to share Jacob Settle's lonely vigil in his cottage on the moor.
10150-8{_}2331{_}I judged that if he got to sleep before sunset he would wake well before midnight, and so, just as the bells of the city were striking eleven, I stood opposite his door armed with a bag, in which were my supper, an extra large flask, a couple of candles, and a book.
10150-8{_}2332{_}The moonlight was bright, and flooded the whole moor, till it was almost as light as day; but ever and anon black clouds drove across the sky, and made a darkness which by comparison seemed almost tangible.
10150-8{_}2333{_}I opened the door softly, and entered without waking Jacob, who lay asleep with his white face upward.
10150-8{_}2334{_}He was still, and again bathed in sweat.
10150-8{_}2335{_}I tried to imagine what visions were passing before those closed eyes which could bring with them the misery and woe which were stamped on the face, but fancy failed me, and I waited for the awakening.
10150-8{_}2336{_}It came suddenly, and in a fashion which touched me to the quick, for the hollow groan that broke from the man's white lips as he half arose and sank back was manifestly the realisation or completion of some train of thought which had gone before.
10150-8{_}2337{_}'If this be dreaming,' said I to myself, 'then it must be based on some very terrible reality.
10150-8{_}2338{_}What can have been that unhappy fact that he spoke of?'
10150-8{_}2339{_}While I thus spoke, he realised that I was with him.
10150-8{_}2340{_}It struck me as strange that he had no period of that doubt as to whether dream or reality surrounded him which commonly marks an expected environment of waking men.
10150-8{_}2341{_}With a positive cry of joy, he seized my hand and held it in his two wet, trembling hands, as a frightened child clings on to someone whom it loves.
10150-8{_}2342{_}I tried to soothe him:  'There, there!
10150-8{_}2343{_}it is all right.
10150-8{_}2344{_}I have come to stay with you tonight, and together we will try to fight this evil dream.'
10150-8{_}2345{_}He let go my hand suddenly, and sank back on his bed and covered his eyes with his hands.
10150-8{_}2346{_}'Fight it?--the evil dream!
10150-8{_}2347{_}Ah!
10150-8{_}2348{_}no, sir, no!
10150-8{_}2349{_}No mortal power can fight that dream, for it comes from God--and is burned in here;' and he beat upon his forehead.
10150-8{_}2350{_}Then he went on:  'It is the same dream, ever the same, and yet it grows in its power to torture me every time it comes.'
10150-8{_}2351{_}'What is the dream?'
10150-8{_}2352{_}I asked, thinking that the speaking of it might give him some relief, but he shrank away from me, and after a long pause said:  'No, I had better not tell it.
10150-8{_}2353{_}It may not come again.'
10150-8{_}2354{_}There was manifestly something to conceal from me--something that lay behind the dream, so I answered:  'All right.
10150-8{_}2355{_}I hope you have seen the last of it.
10150-8{_}2356{_}But if it should come again, you will tell me, will you not?
10150-8{_}2357{_}I ask, not out of curiosity, but because I think it may relieve you to speak.'
10150-8{_}2358{_}He answered with what I thought was almost an undue amount of solemnity:  'If it comes again, I shall tell you all.'
10150-8{_}2359{_}Then I tried to get his mind away from the subject to more mundane things, so I produced supper, and made him share it with me, including the contents of the flask.
10150-8{_}2360{_}After a little he braced up, and when I lit my cigar, having given him another, we smoked a full hour, and talked of many things.
10150-8{_}2361{_}Little by little the comfort of his body stole over his mind, and I could see sleep laying her gentle hands on his eyelids.
10150-8{_}2362{_}He felt it, too, and told me that now he felt all right, and I might safely leave him; but I told him that, right or wrong, I was going to see in the daylight.
10150-8{_}2363{_}So I lit my other candle, and began to read as he fell asleep.
10150-8{_}2364{_}By degrees I got interested in my book, so interested that presently I was startled by its dropping out of my hands.
10150-8{_}2365{_}I looked and saw that Jacob was still asleep, and I was rejoiced to see that there was on his face a look of unwonted happiness, while his lips seemed to move with unspoken words.
10150-8{_}2366{_}Then I turned to my work again, and again woke, but this time to feel chilled to my very marrow by hearing the voice from the bed beside me:  'Not with those red hands!
10150-8{_}2367{_}Never!
10150-8{_}2368{_}never!'
10150-8{_}2369{_}On looking at him, I found that he was still asleep.
10150-8{_}2370{_}He woke, however, in an instant, and did not seem surprised to see me; there was again that strange apathy as to his surroundings.
10150-8{_}2371{_}Then I said:  'Settle, tell me your dream.
10150-8{_}2372{_}You may speak freely, for I shall hold your confidence sacred.
10150-8{_}2373{_}While we both live I shall never mention what you may choose to tell me.'
10150-8{_}2374{_}He replied:  'I said I would; but I had better tell you first what goes before the dream, that you may understand.
10150-8{_}2375{_}I was a schoolmaster when I was a very young man; it was only a parish school in a little village in the West Country.
10150-8{_}2376{_}No need to mention any names.
10150-8{_}2377{_}Better not.
10150-8{_}2378{_}I was engaged to be married to a young girl whom I loved and almost reverenced.
10150-8{_}2379{_}It was the old story.
10150-8{_}2380{_}While we were waiting for the time when we could afford to set up house together, another man came along.
10150-8{_}2381{_}He was nearly as young as I was, and handsome, and a gentleman, with all a gentleman's attractive ways for a woman of our class.
10150-8{_}2382{_}He would go fishing, and she would meet him while I was at my work in school.
10150-8{_}2383{_}I reasoned with her and implored her to give him up.
10150-8{_}2384{_}I offered to get married at once and go away and begin the world in a strange country; but she would not listen to anything I could say, and I could see that she was infatuated with him.
10150-8{_}2385{_}Then I took it on myself to meet the man and ask him to deal well with the girl, for I thought he might mean honestly by her, so that there might be no talk or chance of talk on the part of others.
10150-8{_}2386{_}I went where I should meet him with none by, and we met!'
10150-8{_}2387{_}Here Jacob Settle had to pause, for something seemed to rise in his throat, and he almost gasped for breath.
10150-8{_}2388{_}Then he went on:  'Sir, as God is above us, there was no selfish thought in my heart that day, I loved my pretty Mabel too well to be content with a part of her love, and I had thought of my own unhappiness too often not to have come to realise that, whatever might come to her, my hope was gone.
10150-8{_}2389{_}He was insolent to me--you, sir, who are a gentleman, cannot know, perhaps, how galling can be the insolence of one who is above you in station--but I bore with that.
10150-8{_}2390{_}I implored him to deal well with the girl, for what might be only a pastime of an idle hour with him might be the breaking of her heart.
10150-8{_}2391{_}For I never had a thought of her truth, or that the worst of harm could come to her--it was only the unhappiness to her heart I feared.
10150-8{_}2392{_}But when I asked him when he intended to marry her his laughter galled me so that I lost my temper and told him that I would not stand by and see her life made unhappy.
10150-8{_}2393{_}Then he grew angry too, and in his anger said such cruel things of her that then and there I swore he should not live to do her harm.
10150-8{_}2394{_}God knows how it came about, for in such moments of passion it is hard to remember the steps from a word to a blow, but I found myself standing over his dead body, with my hands crimson with the blood that welled from his torn throat.
10150-8{_}2395{_}We were alone and he was a stranger, with none of his kin to seek for him and murder does not always out--not all at once.
10150-8{_}2396{_}His bones may be whitening still, for all I know, in the pool of the river where I left him.
10150-8{_}2397{_}No one suspected his absence, or why it was, except my poor Mabel, and she dared not speak.
10150-8{_}2398{_}But it was all in vain, for when I came back again after an absence of months--for I could not live in the place--I learned that her shame had come and that she had died in it.
10150-8{_}2399{_}Hitherto I had been borne up by the thought that my ill deed had saved her future, but now, when I learned that I had been too late, and that my poor love was smirched with that man's sin, I fled away with the sense of my useless guilt upon me more heavily than I could bear.
10150-8{_}2400{_}Ah!
10150-8{_}2401{_}sir, you that have not done such a sin don't know what it is to carry it with you.
10150-8{_}2402{_}You may think that custom makes it easy to you, but it is not so.
10150-8{_}2403{_}It grows and grows with every hour, till it becomes intolerable, and with it growing, too, the feeling that you must for ever stand outside Heaven.
10150-8{_}2404{_}You don't know what that means, and I pray God that you never may.
10150-8{_}2405{_}Ordinary men, to whom all things are possible, don't often, if ever, think of Heaven.
10150-8{_}2406{_}It is a name, and nothing more, and they are content to wait and let things be, but to those who are doomed to be shut out for ever you cannot think what it means, you cannot guess or measure the terrible endless longing to see the gates opened, and to be able to join the white figures within.
10150-8{_}2407{_}'And this brings me to my dream.
10150-8{_}2408{_}It seemed that the portal was before me, with great gates of massive steel with bars of the thickness of a mast, rising to the very clouds, and so close that between them was just a glimpse of a crystal grotto, on whose shining walls were figured many white-clad forms with faces radiant with joy.
10150-8{_}2409{_}When I stood before the gate my heart and my soul were so full of rapture and longing that I forgot.
10150-8{_}2410{_}And there stood at the gate two mighty angels with sweeping wings, and, oh!
10150-8{_}2411{_}so stern of countenance.
10150-8{_}2412{_}They held each in one hand a flaming sword, and in the other the latchet, which moved to and fro at their lightest touch.
10150-8{_}2413{_}Nearer were figures all draped in black, with heads covered so that only the eyes were seen, and they handed to each who came white garments such as the angels wear.
10150-8{_}2414{_}A low murmur came that told that all should put on their own robes, and without soil, or the angels would not pass them in, but would smite them down with the flaming swords.
10150-8{_}2415{_}I was eager to don my own garment, and hurriedly threw it over me and stepped swiftly to the gate; but it moved not, and the angels, loosing the latchet, pointed to my dress, I looked down, and was aghast, for the whole robe was smeared with blood.
10150-8{_}2416{_}My hands were red; they glittered with the blood that dripped from them as on that day by the river bank.
10150-8{_}2417{_}And then the angels raised their flaming swords to smite me down, and the horror was complete--I awoke.
10150-8{_}2418{_}Again, and again, and again, that awful dream comes to me.
10150-8{_}2419{_}I never learn from the experience, I never remember, but at the beginning the hope is ever there to make the end more appalling; and I know that the dream does not come out of the common darkness where the dreams abide, but that it is sent from God as a punishment!
10150-8{_}2420{_}Never, never shall I be able to pass the gate, for the soil on the angel garments must ever come from these bloody hands!'
10150-8{_}2421{_}I listened as in a spell as Jacob Settle spoke.
10150-8{_}2422{_}There was something so far away in the tone of his voice--something so dreamy and mystic in the eyes that looked as if through me at some spirit beyond--something so lofty in his very diction and in such marked contrast to his workworn clothes and his poor surroundings that I wondered if the whole thing were not a dream.
10150-8{_}2423{_}We were both silent for a long time.
10150-8{_}2424{_}I kept looking at the man before me in growing wonderment.
10150-8{_}2425{_}Now that his confession had been made, his soul, which had been crushed to the very earth, seemed to leap back again to uprightness with some resilient force.
10150-8{_}2426{_}I suppose I ought to have been horrified with his story, but, strange to say, I was not.
10150-8{_}2427{_}It certainly is not pleasant to be made the recipient of the confidence of a murderer, but this poor fellow seemed to have had, not only so much provocation, but so much self-denying purpose in his deed of blood that I did not feel called upon to pass judgment upon him.
10150-8{_}2428{_}My purpose was to comfort, so I spoke out with what calmness I could, for my heart was beating fast and heavily:  'You need not despair, Jacob Settle.
10150-8{_}2429{_}God is very good, and His mercy is great.
10150-8{_}2430{_}Live on and work on in the hope that some day you may feel that you have atoned for the past.'
10150-8{_}2431{_}Here I paused, for I could see that deep, natural sleep this time, was creeping upon him.
10150-8{_}2432{_}'Go to sleep,' I said; 'I shall watch with you here and we shall have no more evil dreams tonight.'
10150-8{_}2433{_}He made an effort to pull himself together, and answered:  'I don't know how to thank you for your goodness to me this night, but I think you had best leave me now.
10150-8{_}2434{_}I'll try and sleep this out; I feel a weight off my mind since I have told you all.
10150-8{_}2435{_}If there's anything of the man left in me, I must try and fight out life alone.'
10150-8{_}2436{_}'I'll go tonight, as you wish it,' I said; 'but take my advice, and do not live in such a solitary way.
10150-8{_}2437{_}Go among men and women; live among them.
10150-8{_}2438{_}Share their joys and sorrows, and it will help you to forget.
10150-8{_}2439{_}This solitude will make you melancholy mad.'
10150-8{_}2440{_}'I will!'
10150-8{_}2441{_}he answered, half unconsciously, for sleep was overmastering him.
10150-8{_}2442{_}I turned to go, and he looked after me.
10150-8{_}2443{_}When I had touched the latch I dropped it, and, coming back to the bed, held out my hand.
10150-8{_}2444{_}He grasped it with both his as he rose to a sitting posture, and I said my goodnight, trying to cheer him:  'Heart, man, heart!
10150-8{_}2445{_}There is work in the world for you to do, Jacob Settle.
10150-8{_}2446{_}You can wear those white robes yet and pass through that gate of steel!'
10150-8{_}2447{_}Then I left him.
10150-8{_}2448{_}A week after I found his cottage deserted, and on asking at the works was told that he had 'gone north', no one exactly knew whither.
10150-8{_}2449{_}Two years afterwards, I was staying for a few days with my friend Dr. Munro in Glasgow.
10150-8{_}2450{_}He was a busy man, and could not spare much time for going about with me, so I spent my days in excursions to the Trossachs and Loch Katrine and down the Clyde.
10150-8{_}2451{_}On the second last evening of my stay I came back somewhat later than I had arranged, but found that my host was late too.
10150-8{_}2452{_}The maid told me that he had been sent for to the hospital--a case of accident at the gas-works, and the dinner was postponed an hour; so telling her I would stroll down to find her master and walk back with him, I went out.
10150-8{_}2453{_}At the hospital I found him washing his hands preparatory to starting for home.
10150-8{_}2454{_}Casually, I asked him what his case was.
10150-8{_}2455{_}'Oh, the usual thing!
10150-8{_}2456{_}A rotten rope and men's lives of no account.
10150-8{_}2457{_}Two men were working in a gasometer, when the rope that held their scaffolding broke.
10150-8{_}2458{_}It must have occurred just before the dinner hour, for no one noticed their absence till the men had returned.
10150-8{_}2459{_}There was about seven feet of water in the gasometer, so they had a hard fight for it, poor fellows.
10150-8{_}2460{_}However, one of them was alive, just alive, but we have had a hard job to pull him through.
10150-8{_}2461{_}It seems that he owes his life to his mate, for I have never heard of greater heroism.
10150-8{_}2462{_}They swam together while their strength lasted, but at the end they were so done up that even the lights above, and the men slung with ropes, coming down to help them, could not keep them up.
10150-8{_}2463{_}But one of them stood on the bottom and held up his comrade over his head, and those few breaths made all the difference between life and death.
10150-8{_}2464{_}They were a shocking sight when they were taken out, for that water is like a purple dye with the gas and the tar.
10150-8{_}2465{_}The man upstairs looked as if he had been washed in blood.
10150-8{_}2466{_}Ugh!'
10150-8{_}2467{_}'And the other?'
10150-8{_}2468{_}'Oh, he's worse still.
10150-8{_}2469{_}But he must have been a very noble fellow.
10150-8{_}2470{_}That struggle under the water must have been fearful; one can see that by the way the blood has been drawn from the extremities.
10150-8{_}2471{_}It makes the idea of the _Stigmata_ possible to look at him.
10150-8{_}2472{_}Resolution like this could, you would think, do anything in the world.
10150-8{_}2473{_}Ay!
10150-8{_}2474{_}it might almost unbar the gates of Heaven.
10150-8{_}2475{_}Look here, old man, it is not a very pleasant sight, especially just before dinner, but you are a writer, and this is an odd case.
10150-8{_}2476{_}Here is something you would not like to miss, for in all human probability you will never see anything like it again.'
10150-8{_}2477{_}While he was speaking he had brought me into the mortuary of the hospital.
10150-8{_}2478{_}On the bier lay a body covered with a white sheet, which was wrapped close round it.
10150-8{_}2479{_}'Looks like a chrysalis, don't it?
10150-8{_}2480{_}I say, Jack, if there be anything in the old myth that a soul is typified by a butterfly, well, then the one that this chrysalis sent forth was a very noble specimen and took all the sunlight on its wings.
10150-8{_}2481{_}See here!'
10150-8{_}2482{_}He uncovered the face.
10150-8{_}2483{_}Horrible, indeed, it looked, as though stained with blood.
10150-8{_}2484{_}But I knew him at once, Jacob Settle!
10150-8{_}2485{_}My friend pulled the winding sheet further down.
10150-8{_}2486{_}The hands were crossed on the purple breast as they had been reverently placed by some tender-hearted person.
10150-8{_}2487{_}As I saw them my heart throbbed with a great exultation, for the memory of his harrowing dream rushed across my mind.
10150-8{_}2488{_}There was no stain now on those poor, brave hands, for they were blanched white as snow.
10150-8{_}2489{_}And somehow as I looked I felt that the evil dream was all over.
10150-8{_}2490{_}That noble soul had won a way through the gate at last.
10150-8{_}2491{_}The white robe had now no stain from the hands that had put it on.
10150-8{_}2492{_}Crooken Sands   Mr Arthur Fernlee Markam, who took what was known as the Red House above the Mains of Crooken, was a London merchant, and being essentially a cockney, thought it necessary when he went for the summer holidays to Scotland to provide an entire rig-out as a Highland chieftain, as manifested in chromolithographs and on the music-hall stage.
10150-8{_}2493{_}He had once seen in the Empire the Great Prince--'The Bounder King'--bring down the house by appearing as 'The MacSlogan of that Ilk,' and singing the celebrated Scotch song, 'There's naething like haggis to mak a mon dry!'
10150-8{_}2494{_}and he had ever since preserved in his mind a faithful image of the picturesque and warlike appearance which he presented.
10150-8{_}2495{_}Indeed, if the true inwardness of Mr. Markam's mind on the subject of his selection of Aberdeenshire as a summer resort were known, it would be found that in the foreground of the holiday locality which his fancy painted stalked the many hued figure of the MacSlogan of that Ilk.
10150-8{_}2496{_}However, be this as it may, a very kind fortune--certainly so far as external beauty was concerned--led him to the choice of Crooken Bay.
10150-8{_}2497{_}It is a lovely spot, between Aberdeen and Peterhead, just under the rock-bound headland whence the long, dangerous reefs known as The Spurs run out into the North Sea.
10150-8{_}2498{_}Between this and the 'Mains of Crooken'--a village sheltered by the northern cliffs--lies the deep bay, backed with a multitude of bent-grown dunes where the rabbits are to be found in thousands.
10150-8{_}2499{_}Thus at either end of the bay is a rocky promontory, and when the dawn or the sunset falls on the rocks of red syenite the effect is very lovely.
10150-8{_}2500{_}The bay itself is floored with level sand and the tide runs far out, leaving a smooth waste of hard sand on which are dotted here and there the stake nets and bag nets of the salmon fishers.
10150-8{_}2501{_}At one end of the bay there is a little group or cluster of rocks whose heads are raised something above high water, except when in rough weather the waves come over them green.
10150-8{_}2502{_}At low tide they are exposed down to sand level; and here is perhaps the only little bit of dangerous sand on this part of the eastern coast.
10150-8{_}2503{_}Between the rocks, which are apart about some fifty feet, is a small quicksand, which, like the Goodwins, is dangerous only with the incoming tide.
10150-8{_}2504{_}It extends outwards till it is lost in the sea, and inwards till it fades away in the hard sand of the upper beach.
10150-8{_}2505{_}On the slope of the hill which rises beyond the dunes, midway between the Spurs and the Port of Crooken, is the Red House.
10150-8{_}2506{_}It rises from the midst of a clump of fir-trees which protect it on three sides, leaving the whole sea front open.
10150-8{_}2507{_}A trim old-fashioned garden stretches down to the roadway, on crossing which a grassy path, which can be used for light vehicles, threads a way to the shore, winding amongst the sand hills.
10150-8{_}2508{_}When the Markam family arrived at the Red House after their thirty-six hours of pitching on the Aberdeen steamer _Ban Righ_ from Blackwall, with the subsequent train to Yellon and drive of a dozen miles, they all agreed that they had never seen a more delightful spot.
10150-8{_}2509{_}The general satisfaction was more marked as at that very time none of the family were, for several reasons, inclined to find favourable anything or any place over the Scottish border.
10150-8{_}2510{_}Though the family was a large one, the prosperity of the business allowed them all sorts of personal luxuries, amongst which was a wide latitude in the way of dress.
10150-8{_}2511{_}The frequency of the Markam girls' new frocks was a source of envy to their bosom friends and of joy to themselves.
10150-8{_}2512{_}Arthur Fernlee Markam had not taken his family into his confidence regarding his new costume.
10150-8{_}2513{_}He was not quite certain that he should be free from ridicule, or at least from sarcasm, and as he was sensitive on the subject, he thought it better to be actually in the suitable environment before he allowed the full splendour to burst upon them.
10150-8{_}2514{_}He had taken some pains, to insure the completeness of the Highland costume.
10150-8{_}2515{_}For the purpose he had paid many visits to 'The Scotch All-Wool Tartan Clothing Mart' which had been lately established in Copthall-court by the Messrs. MacCallum More and Roderick MacDhu.
10150-8{_}2516{_}He had anxious consultations with the head of the firm--MacCallum as he called himself, resenting any such additions as 'Mr.'
10150-8{_}2517{_}or 'Esquire.'
10150-8{_}2518{_}The known stock of buckles, buttons, straps, brooches and ornaments of all kinds were examined in critical detail; and at last an eagle's feather of sufficiently magnificent proportions was discovered, and the equipment was complete.
10150-8{_}2519{_}It was only when he saw the finished costume, with the vivid hues of the tartan seemingly modified into comparative sobriety by the multitude of silver fittings, the cairngorm brooches, the philibeg, dirk and sporran that he was fully and absolutely satisfied with his choice.
10150-8{_}2520{_}At first he had thought of the Royal Stuart dress tartan, but abandoned it on the MacCallum pointing out that if he should happen to be in the neighbourhood of Balmoral it might lead to complications.
10150-8{_}2521{_}The MacCallum, who, by the way, spoke with a remarkable cockney accent, suggested other plaids in turn; but now that the other question of accuracy had been raised, Mr. Markam foresaw difficulties if he should by chance find himself in the locality of the clan whose colours he had usurped.
10150-8{_}2522{_}The MacCallum at last undertook to have, at Markam's expense, a special pattern woven which would not be exactly the same as any existing tartan, though partaking of the characteristics of many.
10150-8{_}2523{_}It was based on the Royal Stuart, but contained suggestions as to simplicity of pattern from the Macalister and Ogilvie clans, and as to neutrality of colour from the clans of Buchanan, Macbeth, Chief of Macintosh and Macleod.
10150-8{_}2524{_}When the specimen had been shown to Markam he had feared somewhat lest it should strike the eye of his domestic circle as gaudy; but as Roderick MacDhu fell into perfect ecstasies over its beauty he did not make any objection to the completion of the piece.
10150-8{_}2525{_}He thought, and wisely, that if a genuine Scotchman like MacDhu liked it, it must be right--especially as the junior partner was a man very much of his own build and appearance.
10150-8{_}2526{_}When the MacCallum was receiving his cheque--which, by the way, was a pretty stiff one--he remarked:  'I've taken the liberty of having some more of the stuff woven in case you or any of your friends should want it.'
10150-8{_}2527{_}Markam was gratified, and told him that he should be only too happy if the beautiful stuff which they had originated between them should become a favourite, as he had no doubt it would in time.
10150-8{_}2528{_}He might make and sell as much as he would.
10150-8{_}2529{_}Markam tried the dress on in his office one evening after the clerks had all gone home.
10150-8{_}2530{_}He was pleased, though a little frightened, at the result.
10150-8{_}2531{_}The MacCallum had done his work thoroughly, and there was nothing omitted that could add to the martial dignity of the wearer.
10150-8{_}2532{_}'I shall not, of course, take the claymore and the pistols with me on ordinary occasions,' said Markam to himself as he began to undress.
10150-8{_}2533{_}He determined that he would wear the dress for the first time on landing in Scotland, and accordingly on the morning when the _Ban Righ_ was hanging off the Girdle Ness lighthouse, waiting for the tide to enter the port of Aberdeen, he emerged from his cabin in all the gaudy splendour of his new costume.
10150-8{_}2534{_}The first comment he heard was from one of his own sons, who did not recognise him at first.
10150-8{_}2535{_}'Here's a guy!
10150-8{_}2536{_}Great Scott!
10150-8{_}2537{_}It's the governor!'
10150-8{_}2538{_}And the boy fled forthwith and tried to bury his laughter under a cushion in the saloon.
10150-8{_}2539{_}Markam was a good sailor and had not suffered from the pitching of the boat, so that his naturally rubicund face was even more rosy by the conscious blush which suffused his cheeks when he had found himself at once the cynosure of all eyes.
10150-8{_}2540{_}He could have wished that he had not been so bold for he knew from the cold that there was a big bare spot under one side of his jauntily worn Glengarry cap.
10150-8{_}2541{_}However, he faced the group of strangers boldly.
10150-8{_}2542{_}He was not, outwardly, upset even when some of the comments reached his ears.
10150-8{_}2543{_}'He's off his bloomin' chump,' said a cockney in a suit of exaggerated plaid.
10150-8{_}2544{_}'There's flies on him,' said a tall thin Yankee, pale with sea-sickness, who was on his way to take up his residence for a time as close as he could get to the gates of Balmoral.
10150-8{_}2545{_}'Happy thought!
10150-8{_}2546{_}Let us fill our mulls; now's the chance!'
10150-8{_}2547{_}said a young Oxford man on his way home to Inverness.
10150-8{_}2548{_}But presently Mr. Markam heard the voice of his eldest daughter.
10150-8{_}2549{_}'Where is he?
10150-8{_}2550{_}Where is he?'
10150-8{_}2551{_}and she came tearing along the deck with her hat blowing behind her.
10150-8{_}2552{_}Her face showed signs of agitation, for her mother had just been telling her of her father's condition; but when she saw him she instantly burst into laughter so violent that it ended in a fit of hysterics.
10150-8{_}2553{_}Something of the same kind happened to each of the other children.
10150-8{_}2554{_}When they had all had their turn Mr. Markam went to his cabin and sent his wife's maid to tell each member of the family that he wanted to see them at once.
10150-8{_}2555{_}They all made their appearance, suppressing their feelings as well as they could.
10150-8{_}2556{_}He said to them very quietly:  'My dears, don't I provide you all with ample allowances?'
10150-8{_}2557{_}'Yes, father!'
10150-8{_}2558{_}they all answered gravely, 'no one could be more generous!'
10150-8{_}2559{_}'Don't I let you dress as you please?'
10150-8{_}2560{_}'Yes, father!'
10150-8{_}2561{_}--this a little sheepishly.
10150-8{_}2562{_}'Then, my dears, don't you think it would be nicer and kinder of you not to try and make me feel uncomfortable, even if I do assume a dress which is ridiculous in your eyes, though quite common enough in the country where we are about to sojourn?'
10150-8{_}2563{_}There was no answer except that which appeared in their hanging heads.
10150-8{_}2564{_}He was a good father and they all knew it.
10150-8{_}2565{_}He was quite satisfied and went on:  'There, now, run away and enjoy yourselves!
10150-8{_}2566{_}We shan't have another word about it.'
10150-8{_}2567{_}Then he went on deck again and stood bravely the fire of ridicule which he recognised around him, though nothing more was said within his hearing.
10150-8{_}2568{_}The astonishment and the amusement which his get-up occasioned on the _Ban Righ_ was, however, nothing to that which it created in Aberdeen.
10150-8{_}2569{_}The boys and loafers, and women with babies, who waited at the landing shed, followed _en masse_ as the Markam party took their way to the railway station; even the porters with their old-fashioned knots and their new-fashioned barrows, who await the traveller at the foot of the gang-plank, followed in wondering delight.
10150-8{_}2570{_}Fortunately the Peterhead train was just about to start, so that the martyrdom was not unnecessarily prolonged.
10150-8{_}2571{_}In the carriage the glorious Highland costume was unseen, and as there were but few persons at the station at Yellon, all went well there.
10150-8{_}2572{_}When, however, the carriage drew near the Mains of Crooken and the fisher folk had run to their doors to see who it was that was passing, the excitement exceeded all bounds.
10150-8{_}2573{_}The children with one impulse waved their bonnets and ran shouting behind the carriage; the men forsook their nets and their baiting and followed; the women clutched their babies, and followed also.
10150-8{_}2574{_}The horses were tired after their long journey to Yellon and back, and the hill was steep, so that there was ample time for the crowd to gather and even to pass on ahead.
10150-8{_}2575{_}Mrs. Markam and the elder girls would have liked to make some protest or to do something to relieve their feelings of chagrin at the ridicule which they saw on all faces, but there was a look of fixed determination on the face of the seeming Highlander which awed them a little, and they were silent.
10150-8{_}2576{_}It might have been that the eagle's feather, even when arising above the bald head, the cairngorm brooch even on the fat shoulder, and the claymore, dirk and pistols, even when belted round the extensive paunch and protruding from the stocking on the sturdy calf, fulfilled their existence as symbols of martial and terrifying import!
10150-8{_}2577{_}When the party arrived at the gate of the Red House there awaited them a crowd of Crooken inhabitants, hatless and respectfully silent; the remainder of the population was painfully toiling up the hill.
10150-8{_}2578{_}The silence was broken by only one sound, that of a man with a deep voice.
10150-8{_}2579{_}'Man!
10150-8{_}2580{_}but he's forgotten the pipes!'
10150-8{_}2581{_}The servants had arrived some days before, and all things were in readiness.
10150-8{_}2582{_}In the glow consequent on a good lunch after a hard journey all the disagreeables of travel and all the chagrin consequent on the adoption of the obnoxious costume were forgotten.
10150-8{_}2583{_}That afternoon Markam, still clad in full array, walked through the Mains of Crooken.
10150-8{_}2584{_}He was all alone, for, strange to say, his wife and both daughters had sick headaches, and were, as he was told, lying down to rest after the fatigue of the journey.
10150-8{_}2585{_}His eldest son, who claimed to be a young man, had gone out by himself to explore the surroundings of the place, and one of the boys could not be found.
10150-8{_}2586{_}The other boy, on being told that his father had sent for him to come for a walk, had managed--by accident, of course--to fall into the water butt, and had to be dried and rigged out afresh.
10150-8{_}2587{_}His clothes not having been as yet unpacked this was of course impossible without delay.
10150-8{_}2588{_}Mr. Markam was not quite satisfied with his walk.
10150-8{_}2589{_}He could not meet any of his neighbours.
10150-8{_}2590{_}It was not that there were not enough people about, for every house and cottage seemed to be full; but the people when in the open were either in their doorways some distance behind him, or on the roadway a long distance in front.
10150-8{_}2591{_}As he passed he could see the tops of heads and the whites of eyes in the windows or round the corners of doors.
10150-8{_}2592{_}The only interview which he had was anything but a pleasant one.
10150-8{_}2593{_}This was with an odd sort of old man who was hardly ever heard to speak except to join in the 'Amens' in the meeting-house.
10150-8{_}2594{_}His sole occupation seemed to be to wait at the window of the post-office from eight o'clock in the morning till the arrival of the mail at one, when he carried the letter-bag to a neighbouring baronial castle.
10150-8{_}2595{_}The remainder of his day was spent on a seat in a draughty part of the port, where the offal of the fish, the refuse of the bait, and the house rubbish was thrown, and where the ducks were accustomed to hold high revel.
10150-8{_}2596{_}When Saft Tammie beheld him coming he raised his eyes, which were generally fixed on the nothing which lay on the roadway opposite his seat, and, seeming dazzled as if by a burst of sunshine, rubbed them and shaded them with his hand.
10150-8{_}2597{_}Then he started up and raised his hand aloft in a denunciatory manner as he spoke:--  '"Vanity of vanities, saith the preacher.
10150-8{_}2598{_}All is vanity."
10150-8{_}2599{_}Mon, be warned in time!
10150-8{_}2600{_}"Behold the lilies of the field, they toil not, neither do they spin, yet Solomon in all his glory was not arrayed like one of these."
10150-8{_}2601{_}Mon!
10150-8{_}2602{_}Mon!
10150-8{_}2603{_}Thy vanity is as the quicksand which swallows up all which comes within its spell.
10150-8{_}2604{_}Beware vanity!
10150-8{_}2605{_}Beware the quicksand, which yawneth for thee, and which will swallow thee up!
10150-8{_}2606{_}See thyself!
10150-8{_}2607{_}Learn thine own vanity!
10150-8{_}2608{_}Meet thyself face to face, and then in that moment thou shalt learn the fatal force of thy vanity.
10150-8{_}2609{_}Learn it, know it, and repent ere the quicksand swallow thee!'
10150-8{_}2610{_}Then without another word he went back to his seat and sat there immovable and expressionless as before.
10150-8{_}2611{_}Markam could not but feel a little upset by this tirade.
10150-8{_}2612{_}Only that it was spoken by a seeming madman, he would have put it down to some eccentric exhibition of Scottish humour or impudence; but the gravity of the message--for it seemed nothing else--made such a reading impossible.
10150-8{_}2613{_}He was, however, determined not to give in to ridicule, and although he had not yet seen anything in Scotland to remind him even of a kilt, he determined to wear his Highland dress.
10150-8{_}2614{_}When he returned home, in less than half-an-hour, he found that every member of the family was, despite the headaches, out taking a walk.
10150-8{_}2615{_}He took the opportunity afforded by their absence of locking himself in his dressing-room, took off the Highland dress, and, putting on a suit of flannels, lit a cigar and had a snooze.
10150-8{_}2616{_}He was awakened by the noise of the family coming in, and at once donning his dress made his appearance in the drawing-room for tea.
10150-8{_}2617{_}He did not go out again that afternoon; but after dinner he put on his dress again--he had, of course dressed for dinner as usual--and went by himself for a walk on the sea-shore.
10150-8{_}2618{_}He had by this time come to the conclusion that he would get by degrees accustomed to the Highland dress before making it his ordinary wear.
10150-8{_}2619{_}The moon was up and he easily followed the path through the sand-hills, and shortly struck the shore.
10150-8{_}2620{_}The tide was out and the beach firm as a rock, so he strolled southwards to nearly the end of the bay.
10150-8{_}2621{_}Here he was attracted by two isolated rocks some little way out from the edge of the dunes, so he strolled towards them.
10150-8{_}2622{_}When he reached the nearest one he climbed it, and, sitting there elevated some fifteen or twenty feet over the waste of sand, enjoyed the lovely, peaceful prospect.
10150-8{_}2623{_}The moon was rising behind the headland of Pennyfold, and its light was just touching the top of the furthermost rock of the Spurs some three-quarters of a mile out; the rest of the rocks were in dark shadow.
10150-8{_}2624{_}As the moon rose over the headland, the rocks of the Spurs and then the beach by degrees became flooded with light.
10150-8{_}2625{_}For a good while Mr. Markam sat and looked at the rising moon and the growing area of light which followed its rise.
10150-8{_}2626{_}Then he turned and faced eastwards and sat with his chin in his hand looking seawards, and revelling in the peace and beauty and freedom of the scene.
10150-8{_}2627{_}The roar of London--the darkness and the strife and weariness of London life--seemed to have passed quite away, and he lived at the moment a freer and higher life.
10150-8{_}2628{_}He looked at the glistening water as it stole its way over the flat waste of sand, coming closer and closer insensibly--the tide had turned.
10150-8{_}2629{_}Presently he heard a distant shouting along the beach very far off.
10150-8{_}2630{_}'The fishermen calling to each other,' he said to himself and looked around.
10150-8{_}2631{_}As he did so he got a horrible shock, for though just then a cloud sailed across the moon he saw, in spite of the sudden darkness around him, his own image.
10150-8{_}2632{_}For an instant, on the top of the opposite rock he could see the bald back of the head and the Glengarry cap with the immense eagle's feather.
10150-8{_}2633{_}As he staggered back his foot slipped, and he began to slide down towards the sand between the two rocks.
10150-8{_}2634{_}He took no concern as to falling, for the sand was really only a few feet below him, and his mind was occupied with the figure or simulacrum of himself, which had already disappeared.
10150-8{_}2635{_}As the easiest way of reaching _terra firma_ he prepared to jump the remainder of the distance.
10150-8{_}2636{_}All this had taken but a second, but the brain works quickly, and even as he gathered himself for the spring he saw the sand below him lying so marbly level shake and shiver in an odd way.
10150-8{_}2637{_}A sudden fear overcame him; his knees failed, and instead of jumping he slid miserably down the rock, scratching his bare legs as he went.
10150-8{_}2638{_}His feet touched the sand--went through it like water--and he was down below his knees before he realised that he was in a quicksand.
10150-8{_}2639{_}Wildly he grasped at the rock to keep himself from sinking further, and fortunately there was a jutting spur or edge which he was able to grasp instinctively.
10150-8{_}2640{_}To this he clung in grim desperation.
10150-8{_}2641{_}He tried to shout, but his breath would not come, till after a great effort his voice rang out.
10150-8{_}2642{_}Again he shouted, and it seemed as if the sound of his own voice gave him new courage, for he was able to hold on to the rock for a longer time than he thought possible--though he held on only in blind desperation.
10150-8{_}2643{_}He was, however, beginning to find his grasp weakening, when, joy of joys!
10150-8{_}2644{_}his shout was answered by a rough voice from just above him.
10150-8{_}2645{_}'God be thankit, I'm nae too late!'
10150-8{_}2646{_}and a fisherman with great thigh-boots came hurriedly climbing over the rock.
10150-8{_}2647{_}In an instant he recognised the gravity of the danger, and with a cheering 'Haud fast, mon!
10150-8{_}2648{_}I'm comin'!'
10150-8{_}2649{_}scrambled down till he found a firm foothold.
10150-8{_}2650{_}Then with one strong hand holding the rock above, he leaned down, and catching Markam's wrist, called out to him, 'Haud to me, mon!
10150-8{_}2651{_}Haud to me wi' ither hond!'
10150-8{_}2652{_}Then he lent his great strength, and with a steady, sturdy pull, dragged him out of the hungry quicksand and placed him safe upon the rock.
10150-8{_}2653{_}Hardly giving him time to draw breath, he pulled and pushed him--never letting him go for an instant--over the rock into the firm sand beyond it, and finally deposited him, still shaking from the magnitude of his danger, high upon the beach.
10150-8{_}2654{_}Then he began to speak:  'Mon!
10150-8{_}2655{_}but I was just in time.
10150-8{_}2656{_}If I had no laucht at yon foolish lads and begun to rin at the first you'd a bin sinkin' doon to the bowels o' the airth be the noo!
10150-8{_}2657{_}Wully Beagrie thocht you was a ghaist, and Tom MacPhail swore ye was only like a goblin on a puddick-steel!
10150-8{_}2658{_}"Na!"
10150-8{_}2659{_}said I.
10150-8{_}2660{_}"Yon's but the daft Englishman--the loony that had escapit frae the waxwarks."
10150-8{_}2661{_}I was thinkin' that bein' strange and silly--if not a whole-made feel--ye'd no ken the ways o' the quicksan'!
10150-8{_}2662{_}I shouted till warn ye, and then ran to drag ye aff, if need be.
10150-8{_}2663{_}But God be thankit, be ye fule or only half-daft wi' yer vanity, that I was no that late!'
10150-8{_}2664{_}and he reverently lifted his cap as he spoke.
10150-8{_}2665{_}Mr. Markam was deeply touched and thankful for his escape from a horrible death; but the sting of the charge of vanity thus made once more against him came through his humility.
10150-8{_}2666{_}He was about to reply angrily, when suddenly a great awe fell upon him as he remembered the warning words of the half-crazy letter-carrier: 'Meet thyself face to face, and repent ere the quicksand shall swallow thee!'
10150-8{_}2667{_}Here, too, he remembered the image of himself that he had seen and the sudden danger from the deadly quicksand that had followed.
10150-8{_}2668{_}He was silent a full minute, and then said:  'My good fellow, I owe you my life!'
10150-8{_}2669{_}The answer came with reverence from the hardy fisherman, 'Na!
10150-8{_}2670{_}Na!
10150-8{_}2671{_}Ye owe that to God; but, as for me, I'm only too glad till be the humble instrument o' His mercy.'
10150-8{_}2672{_}'But you will let me thank you,' said Mr. Markam, taking both the great hands of his deliverer in his and holding them tight.
10150-8{_}2673{_}'My heart is too full as yet, and my nerves are too much shaken to let me say much; but, believe me, I am very, very grateful!'
10150-8{_}2674{_}It was quite evident that the poor old fellow was deeply touched, for the tears were running down his cheeks.
10150-8{_}2675{_}The fisherman said, with a rough but true courtesy:  'Ay, sir!
